The Four Scenarios of an Oath on Eating Fresh or Unripe Dates
Ruling on oath regarding eating dates with intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four scenarios of an oath on eating rutab or busr and identify which are agreed upon and which are disputed within the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Eating a mudhnab containing the sworn type breaks the oath by agreement if predominant matches; conflict arises when predominant is opposite.

This lesson examines the Hanafi rulings on oaths concerning fresh and unripe dates, focusing on the concept of 'mudhnab'—a date with a trace of the opposite type. The matn presents four scenarios, two agreed upon and two contested between Abu Yusuf and the other Hanafi masters (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad). Understanding these scenarios is essential for properly determining when an oath is broken.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sworn item
Determine which type (rutab or busr) the person swore not to eat. This is the baseline for evaluating the eaten item.
2
Classify the eaten item
Is the eaten item pure rutab, pure busr, or a mudhnab (mixed)? The ruling hinges on the presence of the sworn type in any amount.
3
Apply the mudhnab rule
The matn states that hanth occurs if the mudhnab contains the sworn type, regardless of quantity, but the dispute arises when the predominant type differs from the sworn type.
4
Recognize agreed scenarios
When the mudhnab's predominant type matches the sworn type (e.g., swear not to eat rutab and eat a mudhnab that is mostly rutab), all agree hanth occurs.
5
Identify disputed scenarios
When the mudhnab's predominant type is opposite to the sworn type (e.g., swear not to eat rutab but eat a mudhnab that is mostly busr), Abu Yusuf says no hanth, the two companions say hanth. The same applies in reverse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الأيمان
۰۲
باب اليمين في الأكل والشر
أو لا يأكل رطباً ولا بسراً حنث بالمذنب، ولا يحنث بشراء كباسة بسر فيها رطب
أي أو حلف أن لا يأكل
في لا يشتري رطباً، وبسمك في لا يأكل لحماً، أي في حلفه
لأجل العرف استحساناً
أي قليل
حنث بالمذنب [بكسر النون على وزن اسم فاعل من التفعيل وهو الذي في ذنبه قليل بسر أو قليل رطب. (عيني)] أي مطلقاً سواء كان رطباً مذنباً أو بسراً مذنباً، فالصور أربعة اثنتان اتفاقاً، واثنتان مختلف فيهما، الأولى: حلف لا يأكل رطباً فأكل رطباً مذنباً، حنث اتفاقاً، والثانية: حلف لا يأكل بسراً فأكل بسراً مذنباً، يحنث بالاتفاق، الثالثة: حلف لا يأكل رطباً فأكل بسراً مذنباً، لا يحنث عند الطرفين، وقال أبو يوسف: يحنث، الرابعة حلف لا يأكل بسراً فأكل رطباً مذنباً لا يحنث عند أبي يوسف، ويحنث عند الطرفين، فأبو يوسف اعتبر الغالب؛ إذ المغلوب في مقابلته كالمعدوم عرفاً، فإن الذي عامته رطب يسمى رطباً عرفاً وشرعاً، والعبرة للغالب في الأحكام الشرعية، فصار كما إذا كان اليمين على الشراء، ولهما: أن أكل المذنب أكل بسر ورطب، فيحنث به؛ لأنه أكل المحلوف عليه مع زيادة وإن كان قليلاً؛ لأن ذلك القدر كاف للحنث. ولهذا لو ميزه فأكله يحنث، بخلاف شراء البسر؛ لأن الشراء يصادفه جملة فيكون المغلوب تبعاً، والأكل ينقضي شيئاً فشيئاً، فيصادفه وحده، نظيره إذا حلف لا يشتري شعيراً فاشترى حنطة فيها حبات شعير، لا يحنث، ولو حلف لا يأكل شعيراً فأكل حنطة فيها حبات شعير، يحنث؛ لما ذكرنا، وذكر في "الهداية" قول محمد لله مع قول أبي يوسف، وفي بعضها وفي غيرها من "المبسوط" و"الإيضاح وغيرهما مع أبي حنيفة. فإن قيل: لو حلف لا يشرب هذا اللبن فصب فيه ماء والماء غالب لا يحنث، وإن شرب المحلوف عليه مع زيادة، قلنا: الماء يصير مستهلكاً في اللبن ولا يرى مكانه وههنا يرى مكان المحلوف عليه فكان غير مستهلك حال التناول. (مستخلص)
Book of Oaths. Chapter on oath regarding eating and drinking. Or if he swears not to eat fresh dates nor unripe dates, he breaks the oath by eating a date that has a little of the other on its stalk. And he does not break the oath by buying a bunch of unripe dates which has fresh dates in it. That is, or he swore not to eat... in he does not buy fresh dates, and with fish in he does not eat meat, that is in his oath for the sake of custom by istihsan. That is little. He breaks the oath by a 'mudhnab' (with kasr of nun on the pattern of ism fa'il from taf'il, meaning that which has a little unripe or a little ripe on its stalk). That is absolutely, whether it is a ripe date that has a little unripe or an unripe date that has a little ripe. So the scenarios are four: two agreed upon, two disputed. First: he swore not to eat fresh dates and ate a ripe date with a little unripe; he breaks the oath by agreement. Second: he swore not to eat unripe dates and ate an unripe date with a little ripe; he breaks the oath by agreement. Third: he swore not to eat fresh dates and ate an unripe date with a little ripe; he does not break the oath according to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad?), and Abu Yusuf said he breaks it. Fourth: he swore not to eat unripe dates and ate a ripe date with a little unripe; he does not break the oath according to Abu Yusuf, and breaks it according to the two companions. Abu Yusuf considered the predominant because the subordinate in contrast is like non-existent customarily, for that which is mostly ripe is called ripe customarily and legally, and consideration is for the predominant in sharia rulings, so it becomes as if the oath was on purchase. And according to them (the two companions): eating the mudhnab is eating both unripe and ripe, so he breaks the oath because he ate the sworn item with an increase even if little, because that amount is sufficient for breaking. For this reason, if he separated it and ate it, he breaks the oath, unlike purchasing unripe dates because purchase encounters the whole so the subordinate is a follower, and eating passes little by little so it encounters it alone. Its parallel: if he swore not to buy barley and bought wheat containing grains of barley, he does not break the oath, and if he swore not to eat barley and ate wheat containing grains of barley, he breaks the oath due to what we mentioned. And it is mentioned in al-Hidayah that Muhammad's saying is with Allah? with Abu Yusuf's saying, and in some and in others from al-Mabsut and al-Idah and others with Abu Hanifa. If it is said: if he swore not to drink this milk and poured water into it and the water is predominant, he does not break the oath even though he drank the sworn item with an increase. We say: the water becomes consumed in the milk and its place is not seen, but here the place of the sworn item is seen so it was not consumed at the time of ingestion.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning oaths about eating fresh dates (rutab) and unripe dates (busr). The base matn states that if one swears not to eat either, he breaks the oath only by eating a 'mudhnab' — a date with a small amount of the other type attached at its stalk. The commentator explains that this ruling applies absolutely, whether the mudhnab is predominantly fresh or unripe. Four scenarios are then discussed: (1) Swearing not to eat fresh and eating a fresh mudhnab: break by agreement. (2) Swearing not to eat unripe and eating an unripe mudhnab: break by agreement. (3) Swearing not to eat fresh and eating an unripe mudhnab: the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say no break; Abu Yusuf says break. (4) Swearing not to eat unripe and eating a fresh mudhnab: Abu Yusuf says no break; the two companions say break. The dispute hinges on whether the predominant type determines the name. Abu Yusuf argues that custom gives the name to the predominant type, so the subordinate is ignored. The two companions argue that eating any part of the sworn item suffices to break the oath, even if mixed with its opposite. A parallel is drawn with buying vs. eating: when buying, the subordinate is considered a follower; when eating, each piece is encountered individually. A hypothetical objection about milk mixed with water is answered by distinguishing that water becomes fully consumed in milk, whereas the mudhnab's parts remain distinct. The relied-upon position (al-mufta bihi) is not explicitly given here, but the text notes that al-Hidayah attributes Muhammad's view to be with Abu Yusuf, while other sources align Muhammad with Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms
مذنب (mudhnab): A date that has a small amount of the other type (ripe or unripe) on its stalk.
رطب (rutab): Fresh, ripe dates.
بسر (busr): Unripe dates.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, if someone swears not to eat fresh dates (rutab) and eats an unripe date (busr) that has a little fresh on its stalk (busr mudhnab), which of the following is correct?
A) He breaks the oath by consensus.
B) He does not break the oath by consensus.
C) He breaks the oath according to Abu Yusuf but not according to the two companions.
D) He breaks the oath according to the two companions but not according to Abu Yusuf.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling on oaths involving mixed dates depends on whether the predominant type matches the sworn item, with a dispute when it does not.

The Role of Custom (urf) and Predominance (ghaleeb) in Oaths
Ruling on oath regarding eating dates with intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principles of custom (urf) and predominance (ghaleeb) to determine hanth in oaths involving mixed items, and distinguish between eating and buying modes.

Core Idea
Custom gives name to predominant, but eating encounters each morsel individually; buying treats the subordinate as a follower.

This lesson dives into the reasoning behind the intra-Hanafi dispute. Abu Yusuf relies on the custom that an item is named after its majority, so the subordinate is legally non-existent. The two companions argue that eating is a gradual act, so each piece is considered on its own, and any amount of the sworn item suffices. The parallel with buying and the milk-water analogy further clarify the principles.

Guided Path
1
Understand the principle of ghaleeb
In Arabic custom and sharia, an item is named after its predominant component. For example, a liquid mostly milk is called milk, even if water is added.
This is the basis of Abu Yusuf's position.
2
Examine Abu Yusuf's position
Abu Yusuf applies ghaleeb to oaths: if the predominant of the mudhnab is not the sworn item, the subordinate is like non-existent, so no hanth.
He analogizes with purchase where the subordinate is a follower.
3
Examine the two companions' position
They focus on the act of eating: each morsel is separately consumed, so even a trace of the sworn item breaks the oath. The subordinate is not ignored like in a bulk purchase.
They distinguish eating from buying because eating is incremental.
4
Contrast with buying
When buying, the entire lot is acquired at once, so the subordinate is part of the whole. In eating, each piece is encountered alone, so the subordinate stands independently.
The text gives the example of barley and wheat.
5
Analyze the milk-water analogy
The objection: if water is added to milk and becomes predominant, drinking does not break an oath on milk. The answer: water becomes fully consumed (mustahlak) and invisible, whereas in the mudhnab, the parts remain distinct (mara'i).
This shows the importance of visual separability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

أي أو حلف أن لا يأكل
في لا يشتري رطباً، وبسمك في لا يأكل لحماً، أي في حلفه
لأجل العرف استحساناً
أي قليل
حنث بالمذنب [بكسر النون على وزن اسم فاعل من التفعيل وهو الذي في ذنبه قليل بسر أو قليل رطب. (عيني)] أي مطلقاً سواء كان رطباً مذنباً أو بسراً مذنباً، فالصور أربعة اثنتان اتفاقاً، واثنتان مختلف فيهما، الأولى: حلف لا يأكل رطباً فأكل رطباً مذنباً، حنث اتفاقاً، والثانية: حلف لا يأكل بسراً فأكل بسراً مذنباً، يحنث بالاتفاق، الثالثة: حلف لا يأكل رطباً فأكل بسراً مذنباً، لا يحنث عند الطرفين، وقال أبو يوسف: يحنث، الرابعة حلف لا يأكل بسراً فأكل رطباً مذنباً لا يحنث عند أبي يوسف، ويحنث عند الطرفين، فأبو يوسف اعتبر الغالب؛ إذ المغلوب في مقابلته كالمعدوم عرفاً، فإن الذي عامته رطب يسمى رطباً عرفاً وشرعاً، والعبرة للغالب في الأحكام الشرعية، فصار كما إذا كان اليمين على الشراء، ولهما: أن أكل المذنب أكل بسر ورطب، فيحنث به؛ لأنه أكل المحلوف عليه مع زيادة وإن كان قليلاً؛ لأن ذلك القدر كاف للحنث. ولهذا لو ميزه فأكله يحنث، بخلاف شراء البسر؛ لأن الشراء يصادفه جملة فيكون المغلوب تبعاً، والأكل ينقضي شيئاً فشيئاً، فيصادفه وحده، نظيره إذا حلف لا يشتري شعيراً فاشترى حنطة فيها حبات شعير، لا يحنث، ولو حلف لا يأكل شعيراً فأكل حنطة فيها حبات شعير، يحنث؛ لما ذكرنا، وذكر في "الهداية" قول محمد لله مع قول أبي يوسف، وفي بعضها وفي غيرها من "المبسوط" و"الإيضاح وغيرهما مع أبي حنيفة. فإن قيل: لو حلف لا يشرب هذا اللبن فصب فيه ماء والماء غالب لا يحنث، وإن شرب المحلوف عليه مع زيادة، قلنا: الماء يصير مستهلكاً في اللبن ولا يرى مكانه وههنا يرى مكان المحلوف عليه فكان غير مستهلك حال التناول. beginner
6 min
Oath on Eating Meat Does Not Include Fish
Ruling on oath regarding meat excluding fish, with istihsan proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that an oath not to eat meat does not include fish, and distinguish between qiyas and istihsan in this context.

Core Idea
Fish is not 'meat' in oaths because it lacks blood and custom excludes it.

When a person swears not to eat meat, does eating fish break the oath? The Hanafi school, relying on istihsan and custom, says no, unless the swearer specifically intended fish. This lesson examines the proofs for this ruling and the disagreement with other schools.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is eating fish after an oath not to eat meat. The Hanafi ruling is that no hanth (oath-breaking) occurs if the swearer had no intention to include fish.
Intention is key: if he intended fish, then hanth occurs.
2
Locate the dalil for the opposing view
The opposing schools (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali, and reportedly Abu Yusuf) cite Quran 35:12 which calls fish 'lahm' (meat). This is their primary proof that fish is included.
The Hanafi response is that the Quranic usage is metaphorical.
3
Understand the Hanafi istihsan
The Hanafi argument is that 'lahm' literally means flesh that originates from blood. Fish has no blood, so its flesh is deficient in 'meathood'. Also, oaths are interpreted according to custom ('urf), and custom does not consider fish as 'meat'.
Istihsan overrides qiyas because of the blood deficiency and custom.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Some reports attribute the qiyas view to Abu Yusuf. However, the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position in the Hanafi school is the istihsan view.
This shows internal scholarly diversity.
5
Evaluate the practical consequence
The thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference) is that a Hanafi may eat fish after such an oath without sin or expiation, while a Shafi'i would be required to perform kaffarah if he eats fish.
Custom and blood distinction determine the outcome.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبسمك في لا يأكل لحماً : أي لو حلف لا يأكل لحماً فأكل السمك لم يحنث إذا لم يكن له نية، فأما إذا نواه حنث طرياً أو غير طري، والقياس أن يحنث، وهو قول الشافعي ومالك وأحمد وفي "المجمع": وهو قول أبي يوسف أيضاً؛ لأنه سمي في القرآن لحماً، قال الله تعالى: ﴿وَمِنْ كُلِّ تَأْكُلُونَ لَحْماً طَرِيّا (فاطر: ١٢)، والمراد لحم السمك بالإجماع، وجه الاستحسان أن التسمية مجازية؛ لأن اللحم ينشأ من الدم ولا دم في السمك؛ إذ هو من سواكن الماء؛ إذ بين طبيعة الماء والدم مخالفة؛ لأن الماء بارد والدم حار؛ ولهذا أحل أكل السمك من غير ذكاة، = فصار كالجراد، فكان قاصراً في اللحمية ومطلق الاسم يتناول الكامل دون القاصر، ومبنى الأيمان على العرف حتى لو وكل رجلاً بشراء لحم فاشترى لحم سمك لا يلزمه [ تبيين الحقائق: ٤٦٢/٣] (عيني، فتح، مستخلص)
And with fish in 'he does not eat meat': that is, if he swore not to eat meat and ate fish, he does not break the oath if he had no intention. But if he intended it, he breaks it, fresh or not. Analogy would be that he breaks it, and that is the saying of al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad, and in al-Majma': it is also the saying of Abu Yusuf, because it is called meat in the Quran. Allah Most High said: 'And from each you eat fresh meat' (Fatir 12), and what is meant is the meat of fish by consensus. The basis of istihsan is that the naming is metaphorical, because meat originates from blood, and there is no blood in fish, for it is from the still water, for between the nature of water and blood there is opposition, because water is cold and blood is hot; for this reason, eating fish without slaughter is permissible. So it became like locusts, so it is deficient in meathood, and the absolute name applies to the complete, not the deficient, and oaths are built on custom, so that even if a man appointed another to buy meat and he bought fish meat, it is not binding on him. (Tabyin al-Haqa'iq 3/462)
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof, addressing whether an oath not to eat meat includes fish. The Hanafi position (based on istihsan) is that it does not, unless the swearer specifically intended fish. The alternative qiyas (analogy) would include it, as fish is called 'lahm' in the Quran (Fatir 12). The istihsan relies on the fact that the term 'lahm' literally means flesh that originates from blood, and fish has no blood, so its consumption does not require slaughter. Thus, fish is considered deficient in 'meathood' (قاصر في اللحمية). Additionally, oaths are interpreted according to custom ('urf), and customarily fish is not called 'lahm' in the absolute sense. The text notes that this is the position of Abu Hanifa, while Abu Yusuf agreed with the other schools (qiyas) according to some reports. The relied-upon position (al-mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school is the istihsan view.

Key Terms
لحم (lahm): In the context of oaths, it refers to meat that comes from blood-bearing animals, excluding fish and locusts.
استحسان (istihsan): A method of juristic preference where a deviation from strict analogy (qiyas) is made due to a stronger conflicting evidence, here custom and blood deficiency.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a person swears 'I will not eat meat' and then eats fish without intending fish, does he break his oath?
A) Yes, because fish is called meat in the Quran.
B) No, because fish is not considered meat in customary usage.
C) Yes, because all meat-eating oaths include fish.
D) No, because fish has blood.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, oaths are interpreted according to custom, not strict linguistic meaning; thus fish is not included in 'meat' for oaths unless specifically intended.

Custom Determines Meat in Oaths
Ruling on oath regarding meat includes pork, human flesh, liver, tripe

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whether pork, human flesh, liver, and tripe break an oath not to eat meat, and explain how custom (urf) overrides literal meaning.

Core Idea
In oaths, custom rules: what is not ordinarily eaten does not break the oath, even if literally called meat.

When someone swears not to eat 'meat', does eating pork or liver break the oath? Linguistically, these are meat, but Hanafi fiqh gives decisive weight to custom (urf). This lesson unpacks the relied-upon ruling and the reasoning that custom, not literal definitions, governs oaths.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is eating certain items after swearing not to eat 'meat'. State whether the oath is broken (hanth) or not according to the mufta bihi.
The mufta bihi is that no hanth occurs for pork, human flesh, liver, tripe, lung, and spleen.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
Identify the proof cited: custom (urf). The text states 'oaths are built on custom'. Understand that this is a rational proof (dalil aqli) derived from the general principle that custom shapes legal meanings.
The Hanafi school prioritises 'urf over literal definitions in oaths.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah (how the proof yields the ruling)
Explain how custom leads to the ruling: since pork and human flesh are not customarily eaten, they are not included in the common understanding of 'meat' in oaths. Similarly, liver, tripe, etc., are not called 'meat' in everyday language.
The literal meaning is overridden because the oath-taker's intent is understood according to 'urf.
4
Note the ikhtilaf (disputes)
Identify that the people of Kufa (earlier Hanafis) considered liver and tripe as meat (hanth occurs). Also note that al-Shafi'i and Ahmad agree on liver and tripe (no hanth), but their position on pork is not stated here.
The later Hanafi position (mufta bihi) follows 'urf, rejecting the Kufan view.
5
Evaluate the tarjih (preferred view)
The text labels the custom-based view as 'al-sahih' (the correct one) and states 'upon this is the fatwa'. Recognize that this is the operative ruling in the school.
The authority weight is fatawa mutakhkhirin (later fatwas).

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof concerning whether various items are considered 'meat' for oath purposes. The text first mentions that pork, human flesh, liver, and tripe are meat in reality, implying that an oath not to eat meat would be broken by eating them. However, the commentator then states the 'sahih' (correct) view: that pork and human flesh do not cause breaking because eating them is not customary ('urf). Lung and spleen are also not considered meat in our school, except the lung? The text is unclear. The relied-upon position is that liver and tripe are not meat, and pork and human flesh are not included due to custom.

عرف (urf): Custom; the established practice in a community that shapes the meaning of terms in oaths.

Key Takeaway
Custom (urf) overrides literal definitions in oaths; items not customarily eaten or considered meat do not break an oath not to eat meat.

Oath on Fat: Is Back Fat Included?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on back fat in oath

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on whether back fat breaks an oath on fat, and distinguish it from the position of the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Back fat is fatty meat, not fat — custom determines the oath's scope.

When someone swears not to eat 'fat', does back fat count? This might seem like a simple semantic question, but it reveals a deeper legal methodology: how far does custom (urf) override literal definitions? In this passage, we see an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two chief disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani. Understanding their arguments will sharpen your ability to apply oaths correctly and appreciate the role of usage in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
What is the hypothetical act? The person swears not to eat 'fat' and then consumes back fat. According to Abu Hanifa, he does not break his oath (no hanth). According to the Sahibayn, he does.
The ruling determines the legal consequence: expiation for a broken oath or nothing.
2
State Abu Hanifa’s position and proof
Abu Hanifa argues that back fat is considered 'fatty meat' (lahm samin) in custom, and people use it like meat. The Quranic exception in An'am 146 is 'disconnected' (munqati'), not a proof of inclusion.
Custom overrides literal dictionary meaning in Hanafi usul.
3
State the Sahibayn’s position and proof
The Sahibayn say back fat is fat in reality: it melts like belly fat, and the Quran (An'am 146) mentions the backs' fat as an exception, implying it is normally forbidden fat. The principle of exception (istithna) requires the excepted item to be of the same genus.
They rely on the literal reality and a linguistic principle.
4
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference)
If a person swears not to eat fat, Abu Hanifa permits back fat; the Sahibayn forbid it. The mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa’s view, so in fatwa, back fat does not break the oath.
The difference affects whether one must perform kaffarah for the oath.
5
Note the language nuance (optional)
The passage adds: if the oath is in Persian using 'bi' (fat), back fat is definitely not included according to one opinion. This reinforces the priority of custom and linguistic context.
Shows that rulings can shift with the language of the oath.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبشحم الظهر في "شحماً : [ أي في حلفه"لا يأكل أو لا يبيع أو لا يشتري شحماً. (عيني)] أي لو حلف لا يأكل شحماً فأكل شحم الظهر لا يحنث عند أبي حنيفة. واعلم أن الشحوم أربعة شحم الظهر، وشحم مختلط بالعظم، وشحم على ظاهر الأمعاء، وشحم البطن، ولا خلاف في الحنث في شحم البطن وما على الأمعاء كما لا خلاف في عدم الحنث بما في العظم وشحم الظهر، وقالا يحنث بشحم الظهر أيضاً، قال الشافعي في وجه، وأحمد في رواية؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿إِلَّا مَا حَمَلَتْ و به ظُهُورُهُمَا (الأنعام: ١٤٦)، والأصل في الاستثناء أن يكون من الجنس، ولأنه شحم حقيقة وفيه خاصيته، ألا ترى أنه مذاب كشحم البطن، ولأبي حنيفة: أن شحم الظهر هو اللحم السمين، ويستعمل استعمال اللحوم، وما ذكر في النص استثناء منقطع، وذكر الطحاوي قول محمد مع أبي حنيفة، وقيل: هذا إذا حلف بالعربية، أما لو قال بالفارسية: "بي" لا يقع على شحم الظهر أصلا. (فتح، عيني)
And with the fat of the back in 'fat': that is, in his oath 'not to eat or sell or buy fat'. That is, if he swore not to eat fat and ate back fat, he does not break the oath according to Abu Hanifa. Know that fats are four: back fat, fat mixed with bone, fat on the surface of the intestines, and belly fat. There is no disagreement in breaking the oath for belly fat and that on the intestines, just as there is no disagreement in not breaking for what is in bone and back fat? Wait: the text says 'And the two companions say he breaks the oath with back fat as well.' Al-Shafi'i said in one view, and Ahmad in one narration, due to the verse: 'Except what their backs carry' (An'am 146), and the principle in exception is that it be from the same kind. And because it is fat in reality and has its property, do you not see that it melts like belly fat. And for Abu Hanifa: back fat is fatty meat, and it is used like meats, and what is mentioned in the text is a disconnected exception. And al-Tahawi mentioned that Muhammad's saying is with Abu Hanifa. And it is said: this is if he swore in Arabic; but if he said in Persian 'bi', it does not apply to back fat at all. (Fath, Ayni)
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding whether back fat (shahm al-zahr) is included in an oath not to eat 'fat'. Abu Hanifa holds that it is not, because back fat is considered fatty meat, not fat per se. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, though Tahawi says Muhammad agreed with Abu Hanifa) hold that it is included, relying on the fact that it is called fat in the Quran (An'am 146) and melts like belly fat. The dispute also involves the interpretation of the verse's exception: the Hanafi view sees it as a disconnected exception (mustathna munqati'), while the opponents see it as a connected exception indicating that the backs' fat is included. The relied-upon position is Abu Hanifa's, based on custom (back fat is used like meat). Additionally, if the oath is in Persian, the word 'shahm' does not cover back fat at all.

Key Terms
شحم الظهر (shahm al-zahr): The fat on the back of an animal; considered by Abu Hanifa as fatty meat.
حنث (hanth): The act of breaking an oath (yamin). In this context, whether the consumption of back fat constitutes a violation of the oath.
استثناء منقطع (istithna munqati'): A disconnected exception, where the excepted item is not of the same kind as the general category.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) No, because he intended other fat.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, back fat is legally treated as meat, not fat, and thus does not break an oath against eating fat, based on custom and the interpretation of the Quranic exception as disconnected.

Oath on Meat or Fat Does Not Include Tail
Ruling on oath regarding tail being a third category

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whether eating a tail breaks an oath concerning meat or fat, and explain the basis for classifying the tail as a third category.

Core Idea
The tail is neither meat nor fat—it is a third category, so an oath covering meat or fat does not include it.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in oath-taking: when someone swears not to eat meat or fat, does eating a tail constitute a violation? The Hanafi school treats the tail as a distinct entity, separate from both meat and fat, based on linguistic and customary understanding. We will explore the ruling and the competing view of Ahmad and some Shafi'is.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath and the disputed item
Consider the oath: 'I will not eat meat or fat.' The item in question is the tail (aliyah) of a sheep. Determine whether this falls under the terms of the oath.
2
Classify the tail linguistically and customarily
According to Hanafi scholars, the tail is neither meat (lahm) nor fat (shahm) by common usage. It is a third type (nawʻ thālith). This classification is based on both literal meaning and customary understanding.
3
Apply the principle that oaths are interpreted by common custom
In Hanafi fiqh, the scope of an oath is determined by the common understanding of the terms. Since the tail is not considered meat or fat in ordinary language, it is not included.
4
Consider the opposing view
Ahmad ibn Hanbal and some Shafi'is argue that the tail is included because it is a fatty part of the animal. This represents a legitimate ikhtilaf in the ruling.

Oath on Wheat: Does Bread Break It?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on oath interpretation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the two companions regarding an oath on wheat, and explain the underlying usul principle (literal vs. common metaphorical meaning) that drives the dispute.

Core Idea
An oath on 'wheat' binds only raw grain according to Abu Hanifa; the two companions include bread based on common usage.

Oaths are serious matters in Islamic law, and their precise wording matters. But what counts as 'wheat' when someone swears not to eat it? Does bread made from that wheat count? This lesson unpacks an intra-Hanafi dispute that hinges on a fundamental linguistic principle: When a word has both a literal and a commonly used metaphorical meaning, which should be given priority in interpreting an oath?

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath and the act
The scenario: someone swears 'I will not eat from this wheat' (pointing to a pile of wheat). The person then eats bread made from that wheat. Does the oath break?
The key term is 'this wheat' – the object of the oath is the raw grain.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa rules that eating the bread does NOT break the oath; only nibbling the raw grain breaks it. He gives priority to the literal meaning (haqiqah) of 'wheat' – the raw grain itself.
For Abu Hanifa, bread is not called 'wheat' in a literal sense; it is a transformed product.
3
State the two companions' position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani rule that eating the bread DOES break the oath. They give priority to the commonly used metaphorical meaning (majaz muta'arif) – since bread is the typical product of wheat, the word 'wheat' is commonly understood to include its product.
The companions argue that in common usage, 'wheat' often refers to its product, especially in oaths.
4
Analyze the underlying usul principle
This dispute is built on a difference in usul al-fiqh: When a word has a literal meaning and a commonly understood metaphorical meaning, which takes precedence? Abu Hanifa prefers the literal; the two companions prefer the common metaphorical when it is widespread. The mufta bihi (operative opinion) often follows the companions in cases where custom is strong.
This principle applies beyond oaths to many areas of fiqh where word meaning determines rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالخبز في "هذا البر": أي من حلف لا يأكل من هذا البر، فأكل الخبز منه لا يحنث بل يحنث بقضم الحنطة عند أبي حنيفة، وقالا: إن أكل من خبزها يحنث أيضاً، وهذا مبني على أصل مختلف بينهم، وهو أن اللفظ إذا كانت لا يحنث حقيقة مستعملة ومجاز متعارف فالمجاز المتعارف أولى عندهما، وعند أبي حنيفة: الحقيقة أولى وإن كانت .
And with bread in 'this wheat': that is, whoever swears not to eat from this wheat, and then eats bread from it, he does not break the oath, but he does break by nibbling the grain according to Abu Hanifa. And the two companions said: if he eats from its bread, he also breaks it. And this is based on a principle of disagreement between them: when there is a literal usage and a metaphorical one that is commonly used, the common metaphorical is preferred according to them, while according to Abu Hanifa, the literal is preferred even if...
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about whether an oath not to eat 'this wheat' is broken by eating bread made from it. Abu Hanifa holds that bread is not included; the oath is broken only by eating the raw grain. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that bread is included because the commonly used metaphorical meaning (bread being the typical product of wheat) should be given priority. Abu Hanifa, however, gives priority to the literal meaning (the raw wheat). The relied-upon position is not stated in this fragment, but typically in such cases, the view of the two companions is preferred for fatwa when custom supports it, but here the text is cut off. The principle is about literal vs. metaphorical interpretation in oaths.

Key Terms
حقيقة (haqiqah): Literal meaning of the word, as opposed to metaphorical.
مجاز (majaz): Figurative or extended meaning that is commonly used.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) Abu Hanifa relies on a hadith; the two companions rely on qiyas.
D) The difference is only about the wording of the oath, not about meaning.

Key Takeaway
In oaths, the Hanafi school differs on prioritizing literal vs. common metaphorical meanings, affecting whether bread breaks an oath on wheat.

Rulings on Oaths: Defining Fruit and Condiment
Defines fruit and condiment for oath purposes with intra-Hanafi disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define 'fakiha' and 'idam' for Hanafi oath purposes and apply the definitions to determine whether an oath is broken, including when intra-Hanafi disputes arise.

Core Idea
Not everything people call fruit or condiment breaks an oath; Hanafi definitions rely on exclusive use as treat or subsidiary.

Oaths involving food terms like 'fruit' and 'condiment' require precise legal definitions. The Hanafi school carefully distinguishes between everyday language and legal usage. This lesson examines how Abu Hanifa and his students defined these terms, revealing the importance of intention and customary practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
Recognize that the passage defines two categories (fruit and condiment) for the purpose of oaths. The key question: which foods fall under these categories when the swearer has no specific intention?
The ruling applies only when niyyah (intention) is absent; if intention exists, it governs by consensus.
2
Determine the Hanafi default positions
State Abu Hanifa's definitions: fruit excludes grapes, pomegranates, fresh dates, cucumber, and khiyar; condiment excludes meat and eggs. Identify the reasoning: exclusive use as a treat (fakiha) or subsidiary to bread (idam).
These are the positions in zahir al-riwayah.
3
Note the intra-Hanafi disputes
Record that the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) differ on fruit, considering grapes, pomegranates, and fresh dates as fruit. For condiment, Muhammad includes meat and eggs, and there is a narration from Abu Yusuf supporting this. Also note the dispute on watermelon and cheese.
The dispute on fruit is between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn; on condiment, between the majority (Abu Hanifa and Sahibayn) and Muhammad alone.
4
Apply the consensus rule on intention
Understand that if the swearer had a specific intention when swearing, that intention determines the meaning, and all scholars agree. The disputes only apply when no intention exists.
This is a critical practical point: the mufti should first ask about the swearer's intent.
5
Evaluate the proof for condiment
Analyze the hadith 'Sayyidu idami ahli al-jannati al-lahm' used by Muhammad. The Hanafi response refutes this by arguing that being the 'master' of a category does not make one a member, using the analogy of the caliph being master of non-Arabs without being non-Arab.
This shows the Hanafi method of interpreting ahadith with careful linguistic analysis.

And fruit: apple, watermelon, apricot — not grape, pomegranate, fresh dates, cucumber, and small cucumber (khiyar). Meaning what is enjoyed as fruit after a meal. They are not fruit according to Abu Hanifa. And condiment (idam) is what is used as a dip like vinegar, salt, and oil — not meat and eggs... (unclear) And fruit is a name for what is enjoyed after a meal and before it, meaning to delight. This meaning is established in apples and other fruits except watermelon, about which they differed. From Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi: it is not fruit because what does not have a dry form as fruit does not have its fresh form as fruit. In al-Muhit: dry tree fruits are fruit except watermelon, whose dry form is not customary as fruit in most lands. From al-Shafi'i and Ahmad, there are two views. (Rumuz al-Haqa'iq 356/15) Apple: with damma on ta, shadda on fa, and important ha — in Persian 'sib'. Watermelon: with kasra on ba, shadda on ta — whether green or yellow. Apricot: with kasra on both meems meaning 'zard alu'. (T) Then: 'not grape, pomegranate, fresh dates etc.' — meaning if one swears not to eat fruit and eats grape or pomegranate, he does not break the oath according to Abu Hanifa. They two (Sahibayn) say he breaks the oath with grape, pomegranate, and fresh dates because the meaning of fruit exists in them, and the three (Sahibayn and Shafi'i) held that. And [Abu Hanifa's] view is because the meaning of fruitfulness is deficient in them; they are suitable for nutrition and medicine, so they do not enter into the absolute name of fruit. It is said this is a difference of reasoning. The difference also applies when he had no intention, but if he intended, it is according to his intention by consensus. And his statement: 'cucumber and khiyar' means they are not fruit because they are vegetables by way of being subsidiary; they are placed on tables with vegetables, so one does not break the oath by eating them. (Rumuz al-Haqa'iq 357/1) (Mukhtalasar) And condiment (idam) is what is used as a dip — meaning it is mixed with bread. (Ayni) So if one swears not to eat condiment and has no intention, it applies to liquid that mixes with bread, so he only breaks the oath with a liquid like vinegar because condiment is what is eaten as a subsidiary to bread. The reality of subsidiary consists of two things: one, not eaten alone; second, mixing. So vinegar and oil have this quality because they are not eaten alone, and bread requires them for mixing. Salt is similar because it is not eaten alone usually and dissolves in the mouth, so mixing occurs. And his statement: 'not meat and eggs' — they are not condiment according to both parties because they are eaten alone, so the meaning of subsidiary is not found in them. According to Muhammad: what is predominantly eaten with bread is condiment, and they are customarily eaten as condiment, so they are condiment. The three (Sahibayn and Shafi'i) held this, and Sheikh Abu al-Layth adopted it, and it is a narration from Abu Yusuf because of the hadith: 'The master of the condiment of the people of Paradise is meat.' We said: that is in Paradise, and our discussion is in this world. The fact that it is master of condiment does not necessitate it being condiment, just as it is said the caliph is the master of Arabs and non-Arabs even if he is not from the non-Arabs. And his statement: 'Cheese has three pronunciations: the best is with damma on jim, sukun on ba, and tashdid on nun; second: with two dammas and tashdid on nun; third: with two dammas and tashdid on nun. And there is a difference of Muhammad also regarding it. And this difference applies to these three things when the swearer had no intention; if he intended, it is according to his intention by consensus. (Fath, Ayni) And cheese with jim and ba both with damma and tashdid on nun — in Persian: 'panir'. (Ayni)
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage defines two categories—fruit (fakiha) and condiment (idam)—for the purpose of oaths related to eating. The author begins by stating what constitutes fruit: apple, watermelon, and apricot are fruit, but grape, pomegranate, fresh dates, cucumber, and khiyar are not. The commentary explains that fruit is something eaten as a treat after or before a meal. At Abu Hanifa, grapes and pomegranates are not fruit because they are also used for nutrition and medicine, so they lack the exclusive fruitfulness. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and al-Shafi'i consider them fruit, but the Hanafi position is that the oath is not broken with these items unless the swearer specifically intended them. Watermelon is disputed: some scholars exclude it because its dried form is not customarily eaten as fruit. Cucumber and khiyar are classed as vegetables. Next, condiment is defined as something mixed with bread, like vinegar, oil, and salt. Meat and eggs are not condiment at Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn because they are eaten independently. Muhammad, following a hadith, considers meat a condiment, but the author refutes this by noting the hadith refers to Paradise, not this world. Cheese is discussed linguistically and jurisprudentially: there is also a difference of Muhammad regarding cheese. All these differences apply only when the swearer had no specific intention; intention controls.

Key Terms
فاكهة (fākihah): What is eaten as a treat after or before a meal, typically sweet fruits.
إدام (idām): Something liquid or semi-liquid that is mixed with bread, like oil, vinegar, salt; not eaten alone.
تبعية (taba'iyyah): Subsidiary nature: not eaten alone, and mixed with the main food (bread).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man swears he will not eat 'fakiha' and then eats a pomegranate, does he break his oath according to Abu Hanifa?
A) Yes, because pomegranate is a fruit.
B) No, because pomegranate is not considered fruit for oath purposes.
C) Yes, if he intended fruit.
D) No, unless he specifically intended pomegranate.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi oath rulings, intention (niyyah) is paramount, but in its absence, the legal definitions of 'fruit' and 'condiment' are based on exclusive use as a treat or subsidiary, excluding items with dual purposes.

Timings of Meals in Oaths: Ghadā', 'Ashā', Sahūr
Defines time periods for meal-related oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi time definitions for ghadā', 'ashā', and sahūr, and apply these definitions to determine whether a meal-related oath is broken.

Core Idea
Meal times in oaths are defined by custom: ghadā' dawn-noon, 'ashā' noon-midnight, sahūr midnight-dawn.

When a person swears not to eat a specific meal, the validity of the oath depends on precise time boundaries. This lesson clarifies the Hanafi definitions for ghadā' (lunch), 'ashā' (dinner), and sahūr (pre-dawn meal), along with the conditions of amount and customary food.

Guided Path
1
Identify the meal and its time period
From the text, each meal has a specific interval: ghadā' from dawn to noon, 'ashā' from noon to midnight, sahūr from midnight to dawn.
The hadith about zuhr being one of the two 'ishā' prayers supports 'ashā' starting at noon.
2
Understand the condition of amount
The oath is broken only if more than half-satiety is eaten, because a few bites are not customarily called a meal.
This condition prevents trivial violations.
3
Understand the condition of customary food
The food must be what the people of his locality customarily eat; e.g., milk satiates a Bedouin but not a city dweller.
Custom (urf) is a key determinant.
4
Note the linguistic nuance for sahūr
The vowel on the sin changes meaning: with damma it is the act of eating; with fatha it is the food. This affects interpretation of the oath.
Understanding the Arabic helps in accurate application.
5
Apply to an oath scenario
Given a situation, check time, amount, and customary food to determine if the oath is broken.
All three conditions must be satisfied for the oath to be broken.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(عيني) إن كان بضم السين فهو اسم الأكل في ذلك الوقت، فيصح حمل الأكل عليه بلا تأويل، وإن كان بفتح السين فهو اسم لما يؤكل فيه، فيحتاج لتأويل بتقدير مضاف له أو يجعل معنى التسحر مجازاً وهو مأخوذ من السحر، وهو ما بعد نصف الليل إلى طلوع الفجر، وإطلاقه على ما بعد نصف الليل لقرب السحر. And ghadā': eating from dawn to noon. And 'ashā': from noon to midnight. And sahūr: from midnight to dawn. Ghadā': [If one swears not to eat ghadā' and eats in this time, he breaks the oath (Ayni)] Ghadā' (with fatha) is the food of the morning; similarly 'ashā' (with fatha) is the food after noon. In interpreting ghadā' as eating, it is a broadening. Ghadā' and 'ashā' can mean the act of eating ghadā' and 'ashā', or with a deleted genitive construction meaning 'the food of ghadā' and 'ashā'. So if one swears not to eat ghadā' and eats in this time (i.e., from dawn to noon), he breaks the oath. If he eats before or after, he does not break. The amount that breaks the oath is more than half of satiety because one or two morsels are not called ghadā' customarily. The type of food must be what the people of his town customarily eat; so if he drinks milk to satiety, he does not break the oath if he is a city dweller, but if he is a Bedouin, he breaks. [Rumuz al-Haqa'iq 357/1] And 'ashā' is from noon because after noon is 'ashā'; hence noon (zuhr) is one of the two 'ishā' prayers in the hadith narrated from Abu Huraira that he said: 'The Messenger of Allah led us in one of the two 'ishā' prayers,' and it was interpreted as zuhr. (Fath) Midnight: So if one swears not to eat 'ashā' and eats in this time, he breaks. (Ayni) And sahūr is from [midnight to dawn]: So if one swears not to eat sahūr and eats in this time, he breaks. (Ayni) If (sahūr) is with damma on sin, it is the name of the eating in that time, so understanding the eating is valid without interpretation. If with fatha on sin, it is the name of what is eaten then, so it needs interpretation by deleting a genitive (i.e., 'eating of sahūr') or treating the meaning of 'tasahhur' metaphorically. It is derived from 'sahar' (the time before dawn), which is after midnight until dawn, and it is applied to the period after midnight because of proximity to dawn. [Rumuz al-Haqa'iq 358/1] (Fath)
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage defines the time periods for meals — ghadā' (lunch), 'ashā' (dinner), and sahūr (pre-dawn meal) — as they relate to oaths about eating those meals. Ghadā' is from dawn (fajr) to noon (zuhr). 'Ashā' is from noon to midnight. Sahūr is from midnight to dawn. If someone swears not to eat one of these meals and eats within its defined time, he breaks the oath. The amount that breaks the oath is more than half of satiety, as a few bites are not customarily called a meal. Also, the type of food must be customary in the person's locality. For example, drinking milk to satiety breaks the oath for a Bedouin but not for a city dweller. The commentary also clarifies the linguistic forms: 'ghadā'' and 'ashā'' can refer to the meal itself or the act of eating. The hadith is cited to support that 'ashā' starts at noon. For 'sahūr', the distinction between the vowel sounds affects the interpretation: with damma it is the act of eating, with fatha it is the food, requiring a slightly different reading of the oath.

Key Terms
الغداء (al-ghadā'): The meal eaten from dawn to noon; also the act of eating that meal.
العشاء (al-'ashā'): The meal eaten from noon to midnight.
السحور (al-sahūr): The meal eaten from midnight to dawn; also the act or the food depending on vowel.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Yes, because 3 PM is after noon and before midnight.
B) No, because 'ashā' begins at sunset.
C) Yes, only if he eats more than half-satiety.
D) No, because he ate a full meal, not a snack.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, meal oaths are broken only when eating occurs in the defined time, exceeds half-satiety, and involves customary food.

Intention Cannot Narrow General Oath Phrases
Ruling on specifying intention in general oath phrases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a general oath phrase is not narrowed by a later claim of specific intention, and explain the proof based on iqtida' and the nature of the maṣdar.

Core Idea
General oath phrases apply to all instances; intention cannot restrict them because the verb's implied maṣdar lacks generality.

When someone swears using a general verb like 'eat' or 'wear', can they later claim they only intended a specific item? The Hanafi school firmly says no – such a claim is not accepted at all. This lesson unpacks the ruling, its linguistic proof, and the minority view that differs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
State the legal consequence: the oath applies broadly, and the swearer's claim of specific intention is rejected both in court and in conscience.
The text says 'lam yusaddaq aṣlan' – not believed at all.
2
Examine the Proof (Dalīl)
Understand that the verb implies its verbal noun (maṣdar) by iqtidā', but that implication has no generality (lā ʿumūma lahu). The maṣdar indicates the essence of the act, not specific instances.
This is a linguistic argument rooted in uṣūl al-fiqh.
3
Consider the Intention Principle
Intention can only clarify what is ambiguous in the wording itself. Since the specific object (e.g., bread) is not mentioned, there is no ambiguity to resolve, so intention cannot restrict.
Reference to 'al-niyyah innamā taʿmalu fī al-malfūẓ'.
4
Note the Ikhtilāf
Abū Yūsuf, al-Khaṣṣāf, and al-Shāfiʿī held that he is believed diyānatan (in conscience) because the maṣdar in a conditional context is general and subject to specification.
The majority Hanafi view rejects this.
5
Determine the Muftā Bihī
The relied-upon position is that the swearer is not believed at all (lā qaḍāʾan wa lā diyānatan), especially when the oath involves divorce or manumission.
This is the muftā bihī according to the text's author.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن لبستُ أو أكلتُ أو شربت ونوى شيئاً معيناً لم يصدق أصلاً. إن لبست: أي لو حلف وقال: إن لبست فعبدي حرٌّ أو أكلت أي أو قال: إن أكلت فعبدي حر. (عيني) أو شربت: أي لو قال: إن شربت فعبدي حر، وكذا لو قال: إن نكحت أو اغتسلت فعبدي حر. (ط، عيني) معينا: بأن قال: نويت الحرير في الأول والخبز في "أكلت" والماء في "شربت" ونحوه. (عيني) لم يصدق أصلاً [ يعني لا قضاء ولا ديانة فيحنث بأي شيء أكل أو شرب. (عيني)] أي من حلف إن لبست فعبدي حر، وكذا إن أكلت وشربت فعبدي حر، وقال: نويت شيئاً دون شيء لم يصدق في القضاء ولا في الديانة؛ لأن دلالة هذه الأفعال على هذه الأشياء بطريق الاقتضاء، والمقتضى لا عموم له، ولا يجوز أن ينوي دون ثوب أو أكلاً دون أكل؛ لأن المصدر الثابت في ضمن الفعل دل على الماهية دون الأفراد، فلا يكون عاما قابلا للتخصيص؛ ولأن النية إنما تعمل في الملفوظ ؛ لأنها لتعيين المحتمل، والطعام ونحوه غير مذكور، وعن أبي يوسف محله: أنه يصدق ديانة، وبه أخذ الخصاف والشافعي [ رمز الحقائق: ٣٥٨/١] لأن ذكر الفعل ذكر للمصدر، وهو نكرة في موضع الشرط فيعم، فيقبل التخصيص، ولأنه مذكور تقديراً وإن لم يذكر صريحا إلا أنه خلاف الظاهر، فلا يصدق قضاء فيما إذا كانت يمينه بطلاق أو عتاق، ولا تعلق للقضاء في اليمين بالله تعالى. (مستخلص)
If I wear, or I eat, or I drink, and he intends something specific, he is not believed at all. 'If I wear': meaning if he swears and says 'If I wear, then my slave is free' or 'If I eat' or 'If I drink' similarly. (Ayni) 'Or I drink': meaning if he says 'If I drink, then my slave is free', and likewise if he says 'If I marry' or 'If I wash' etc. (T, Ayni) 'Something specific': as if he says 'I intended silk in the first (i.e., wearing), bread in 'I ate', water in 'I drank'', and similar. (Ayni) 'He is not believed at all': meaning neither in court nor in conscience; so he breaks the oath by anything he eats or drinks. (Ayni) That is, if he swears 'If I wear, my slave is free', and similarly 'If I eat or drink, my slave is free', and says 'I intended something specific', he is not believed in court or in conscience because the indication of these verbs to these things is by way of implication (iqtidā'), and implication has no generality. And it is not permissible for him to intend a specific garment or a specific instance of eating, because the maṣdar implied in the verb indicates the essence (māhiyya) without specifying individuals, so it is not general and subject to specification. Also, intention only functions for what is pronounced, because it is to specify what is probable, and food and the like are not mentioned. From Abu Yusuf in his view: he is believed in conscience, and al-Khassaf and al-Shafi'i adopted this, because mentioning the verb is mentioning the maṣdar, and it is an indefinite in the conditional context, so it is general and can be specified. And also because it is implicitly mentioned even if not explicit, but that is contrary to the apparent, so he is not believed in court when his oath involves divorce or manumission, and there is no court implication in the oath by Allah. (Mukhtalasar)
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage discusses a fundamental principle: if someone makes a general oath (e.g., 'If I eat, my slave is free') and later claims he intended a specific food (e.g., bread only), his claim is not accepted at all — neither in court nor in his conscience. The reason is that general verbs like 'eat' or 'wear' indicate the act by way of iqtidā' (implication), which has no generality; the implied maṣdar is indefinite but only indicates the essence, not specific instances. Intention only operates on what is actually pronounced; since the specific object is not mentioned, intention cannot restrict it. A minority view from Abu Yusuf, al-Khassaf, and al-Shafi'i holds that he is believed in conscience because the verb implies the maṣdar which is indefinite in a conditional context and thus general, allowing specification. However, the majority and the text's author reject this, and in practice, for oaths involving divorce or manumission (which are judicial), he is not believed; for oaths by Allah, there is no court involvement anyway.

Key Terms
الاقتضاء (al-iqtiḍā'): Implication: the maṣdar (verbal noun) is implied by the verb but not explicitly stated; this implication does not have generality.
المصدر (al-maṣdar): The verbal noun (e.g., 'eating') which is implied by the finite verb; it indicates the essence of the action without specifying particular instances.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man swears 'If I eat, my slave is free' and then claims he only intended to avoid eating bread, does his oath break if he eats a date?
A) No, because he intended only bread.
B) Yes, because his claim is not accepted; he breaks the oath by eating anything.
C) Yes, but only if he eats bread.
D) No, because dates are not food.

Key Takeaway
General oath phrases cannot be narrowed by intention because the verb's implied verbal noun has no generality; thus the swearer is not believed at all.

Default Duration in Oaths Using Zaman and Heen
Determining default duration for oaths using zaman and heen

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi default duration for oaths using 'zaman' or 'heen', derive it from the Quranic usage, and distinguish the Shafi'i and Maliki positions.

Core Idea
The default for 'zaman' or 'heen' in oaths is six months, as the middle and customary interpretation.

When someone swears using vague time terms like 'zaman' (time) or 'heen' (period) without specifying a duration, the Hanafi school assigns a default of six months. This ruling is based on the Quranic usage of 'heen' in multiple verses, where it can mean an hour, forty years, or six months. The Hanafi jurists choose six months as a balanced middle ground and because custom supports it. In this lesson, we will examine the evidence, the reasoning, and how it contrasts with other schools.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core legal issue
The passage addresses what default duration applies when an oath uses 'zaman' or 'heen' without a specific intention. Determine the act (swearing an oath) and the specific question (what is the implied time period?).
The key is that the oath-taker did not specify a duration; default rules apply.
2
Examine the linguistic evidence from Quran
The author cites three Quranic verses where 'heen' appears with different meanings: a moment (hour), forty years, and six months. Analyze each verse and note how the word is used in context.
Refer to Quran 30:17 (hour), 76:1 (forty years), and 14:25 (six months with Ibn Abbas's commentary).
3
Apply the Hanafi interpretive principle
The Hanafi school chooses the middle meaning (six months) because it avoids extremism: a moment is too short to be intended in an oath (one could easily abstain), and forty years is practically a lifetime. This is supported by the maxim 'the best of matters is their middle'.
Also consider custom: six months is the customary period that people commonly refer to.
4
Note the inter-madhhab disagreement
Contrast with Shafi'i (one hour) and Maliki (one year). Understand thamarah al-khilaf: practical consequence if the oath-taker speaks after one month or after one year.
The dispute only arises when no specific intention exists; if the oath-taker intended a different period, that intention is followed.
5
State the Hanafi ruling and its scope
The default is six months from the time of the oath. Speaking within six months breaks the oath; after six months, no violation. The rule applies equally to 'zaman' and 'heen', definite or indefinite.
The ruling is from the Zahir al-Riwayah and is the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والزمان والحين إلخ لأن الحين يذكر بمعنى الساعة قال تعالى: ﴿فَسُبْحَانَ اللهِ حِينَ تُمْسُونَ﴾ (الروم:١٧) أي ساعة تمسون، ويطلق على أربعين سنة، قال تعالى: ﴿هَلْ أَتَى عَلَى الْإِنْسَانِ حِينٌ مِنَ الدَّهْرِ﴾ (الإنسان:١)، والمراد أربعون سنة، ويطلق على ستة أشهر، قال تعالى: ﴿تُؤْتِي أُكُلَهَا كُلَّ حِينٍ ﴾ (إبراهيم:٢٥)، قال ابن عباس المسيب له في ستة أشهر، فيحمل عليه؛ لأنه هو الوسط، وخير الأمور أوسطها؛ لأن اللحظة لا يقصد الامتناع عنها باليمين للقدرة على الامتناع بدونها، وأربعون سنة بمنزلة الأبد، ومن يؤمل أن يعيش أربعين سنة. والزمان يستعمل استعمال الحين يقال: ما رأيتك منذ حين ومنذ زمان ويستوي فيه المعرف والمنكر؛ لأن ستة أشهر لما كانت معهودة انصرف العرف إليها، ويعتبر ابتداؤها من وقت اليمين، فلو حلف لا يكلمه الزمان أو الحين أو زمانا أو حيناً ولم ينو فعلى ستة أشهر، فلو كلمه قبل مضي ستة أشهر يحنث وبعده لا، وعند الشافعي: الزمان والحين ساعة، وعند مالك: سنة، والخلاف فيما إذا لم تكن له نية، وأما إذا نوى شيئاً فعلى ما نوى؛ لأنه محتمل كلامه تبيين الحقائق: ٤٨٩/٣] (عيني، فتح)
And 'zaman' and 'heen' etc., because 'heen' is mentioned in the meaning of hour: He said, exalted: 'So glorify Allah when you enter the evening' (30:17), meaning the hour of evening. And it is applied to forty years: He said, exalted: 'Has there come upon man a period of time (heen) from the dahr?' (76:1), meaning forty years. And it is applied to six months: He said, exalted: 'It brings forth its fruit every season (heen)' (14:25), Ibn Abbas said: 'Its fruit every six months.' So it is interpreted accordingly, because it is the middle, and the best of matters is their middle. Because the moment is not intended for abstention in an oath, since it is possible to abstain without it, and forty years is like eternity, and a person hopes to live to forty. And 'zaman' is used like 'heen'; one says: 'I have not seen you since a heen' and 'since a zaman', and the definite and indefinite are equal. Because six months is customary, custom refers to it, and its start is considered from the time of the oath. So if he swears not to speak to him 'the zaman' or 'the heen' or 'a zaman' or 'a heen' and has no intention, then it is six months. If he speaks before six months elapse, he breaks his oath; after, no. And according to al-Shafi'i: 'zaman' and 'heen' mean one hour; according to Malik: one year. The dispute is when he has no intention; if he intended something, then it is according to his intention, because his words are susceptible. (Tabyeen al-Haqa'iq, Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage determines the default duration of oaths using the terms 'zaman' (time) and 'heen' (period). The word 'heen' has various meanings in the Quran: it can mean an hour, forty years, or six months. Hanafi scholars choose six months because it is the middle option, and the best matters are their middle. A moment (hour) is too short to be the object of an oath, as one can easily abstain, and forty years is almost a lifetime. Custom also becomes established around six months. 'Zaman' is used similarly to 'heen'. If the oath-taker has no specific intention, the default period is six months from the time of the oath. If he speaks within six months, he breaks his oath; after that, no. The Shafi'i school holds it as one hour, and the Maliki school as one year. If the oath-taker had a specific intention, that intention is followed (e.g., if he meant a different period).

Key Terms
الحين (al-ḥīn): A time period with multiple possible lengths; default is six months in oaths.
الزمان (al-zamān): Similar to 'heen', default is six months.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Someone swears 'I will not speak to Zayd for a zaman' without specifying. He speaks after 4 months. Does he break his oath according to the Hanafi school?
A) Yes, because the default is six months.
B) No, because the default is one hour.
C) Yes, because the default is one year.
D) No, because the oath is indefinite.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the default duration for an oath using 'zaman' or 'heen' without specific intention is six months, derived from the middle interpretation of Quranic usage and supported by custom.

Oath Attached to a Person's Essence
Ruling on oath regarding owner of specific garment after sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling that an oath attached to a person's essence remains binding even after the object (e.g., garment) is sold, and distinguish cases where the oath is broken from those where it is not.

Core Idea
The oath attaches to the person, not to the object.

Imagine swearing not to speak to the owner of a specific garment. If the owner sells the garment, is the oath still valid? The Hanafi school rules that the oath remains because the prohibition is on the person’s essence (dhat), not on the garment. This lesson examines the reasoning and the consensus behind this ruling, clarifying when the oath is broken and when it is not.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the oath statement
The swearer says: 'I will not speak to the owner of this taylasan (garment).' The act is the oath (yamin) to refrain from speaking to a specific person identified by his ownership of an object.
The object is just a descriptor; the core is the person.
2
Note the change in circumstances
The owner sells the taylasan. Now the person is no longer the owner of that particular garment at the time the swearer speaks to him.
The question: does the change affect the oath?
3
Apply the principle: oath on essence
The Hanafi position, based on consensus, is that the oath is on the person's essence (dhat) because enmity or attachment is towards the person, not the object. Therefore, the oath remains even after the object is sold.
Proof: the text says 'حنث إجماعاً لأن الامتناع لذاته لا للطيلسان'.
4
Determine the ruling
If the swearer speaks to the former owner after the sale, he breaks the oath and must offer expiation (kaffarah). If he speaks to the buyer of the garment, he does not break the oath because that is a different person.
The same rule applies to 'owner of this house' or 'owner of this garment'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يكلم صاحب هذا الطيلسان فباعه فكلمه حنث. والزمان والحين ومنكرهما ستة أشهر. أي لو حلف لا يكلم إلخ. والدهر والأبد. أي الحالف. مبتدأ عطف عليه وهو الموجود، فتكون معاداته لذاته عنده وعندهما يحنث لأجل الإضافة، هذا إذا لم تكن له نية، وأما إذا نوى فعلى ما نوى؛ لأنه نوى محتمل كلامه. هذا الطيلسان وهو معرب "تيلسان"، أبدلوا التاء منه طاء، وهو من لباس العجم قلنسوة مدورة أسود، يلبس فوق العمامة لحمته وسداه صوف، وزنه فيعلان بفتح الفاء والعين وقيل بكسر العين، أي لو حلف "لا يكلم صاحب هذا الطيلسان" فباع الرجل طيلسانه ثم كلمه الحالف، حنث إجماعاً؛ لأن الامتناع لذاته لا للطيلسان، ولهذا لو كلم المشتري لا يحنث والظاهر أن الطيلسان مثال، فإن قوله: صاحب هذه الدار أو صاحب هذا الثوب كذلك؛ لأن الإنسان لا يعادي لأجل الدار وغيرها، بل لمعنى فيه فيراد الذات. (فتح، مستخلص) فباعه: أي باع صاحب الطيلسان طيلسانه. (عيني) حنث إجماعاً لأن الامتناع لذاته لا للطيلسان. (عيني) والزمان والحين إلخ لأن الحين يذكر بمعنى الساعة قال تعالى: ﴿فَسُبْحَانَ اللهِ حِينَ تُمْسُونَ﴾ (الروم:١٧) أي ساعة تمسون، ويطلق على أربعين سنة، قال تعالى: ﴿هَلْ أَتَى عَلَى الْإِنْسَانِ حِينٌ مِنَ الدَّهْرِ﴾ (الإنسان:١)، والمراد أربعون سنة، ويطلق على ستة أشهر، قال تعالى: ﴿تُؤْتِي أُكُلَهَا كُلَّ حِينٍ (إبراهيم:٢٥)، قال ابن عباس المسيب له في ستة أشهر، فيحمل عليه؛ لأنه هو الوسط، وخير الأمور أوسطها؛ لأن اللحظة لا يقصد الامتناع عنها باليمين للقدرة على الامتناع بدونها، وأربعون سنة بمنزلة الأبد، ومن يؤمل أن يعيش أربعين سنة. والزمان يستعمل استعمال الحين يقال: ما رأيتك منذ حين ومنذ زمان ويستوي فيه المعرف والمنكر؛ لأن ستة أشهر لما كانت معهودة انصرف العرف إليها، ويعتبر ابتداؤها من وقت اليمين، فلو حلف لا يكلمه الزمان أو الحين أو زمانا أو حيناً ولم ينو فعلى ستة أشهر، فلو كلمه قبل مضي ستة أشهر يحنث وبعده لا، وعند الشافعي: الزمان والحين ساعة، وعند مالك: سنة، والخلاف فيما إذا لم تكن له نية، وأما إذا نوى شيئاً فعلى ما نوى؛ لأنه محتمل كلامه. (عيني، فتح) ستة خبر المبتدأ، أي لو قال: لا أكلم فلاناً الزمان أو الحين أو زماناً أو حينا، فهو على ستة أشهر من حين حلفه. (ط، عيني) والدهر : مبتدأ بأن قال: لا أكلم فلاناً الدهر بالألف واللام (عيني) والأبد : حتى لو حلف لا أكلم فلاناً الدهر أو الأبد، فاليمين تنعقد على العمر؛ لأن استعمالهما معرفاً لا يكون إلا في العمر، أما الدهر فقال الشاعر: يمضي الدهر والأيام والذنب حاصل وجاء رسول الموت والقلب غافل وكذا الأبد قال عليه: " من صام الأبد فلا صيام له" أي صام عمره كله، وعند الشافعي وأحمد: يقع على ساعة، وعند مالك على سنة.
He does not speak to the owner of this taylasan, then he sold it, then he spoke to him, he breaks the oath. And [the term] al-zaman and al-hin and their indefinite forms [are] six months. Meaning: if he swore not to speak, etc. And al-dahr and al-abad. That is the swearer. [The phrase is] a subject with a conjunctive, and it is the existing entity; so his enmity [is] towards the essence according to him, and according to both of them he breaks the oath because of the addition; this is if he had no intention, but if he intended, then [it is] according to what he intended, because he intended what his speech could bear. This taylasan is an arabicized form of 'taylasan', they replaced the 'ta' with 'ta', it is a garment of the non-Arabs, a round black cap worn over the turban, its weft and warp are wool, its measure is fay'alan with fatha on the fa and 'ayn, or with kasr on the 'ayn. Meaning: if he swore 'I will not speak to the owner of this taylasan', then the man sold his taylasan, then the swearer spoke to him, he breaks the oath by consensus, because the prohibition is for the essence, not for the taylasan. For this reason, if he spoke to the buyer, he does not break the oath. And it appears that the taylasan is an example, because his saying 'owner of this house' or 'owner of this garment' is similar, because a person does not have enmity for the sake of the house or other things, but for a meaning in it, so the essence is intended. (Fath, Mustakhlas) So he sold it: meaning the owner of the taylasan sold his taylasan. ('Ayni) He breaks the oath by consensus because the prohibition is for the essence, not for the taylasan. ('Ayni) And al-zaman and al-hin etc., because al-hin is mentioned in the meaning of hour. Allah says, {So glorify Allah when you enter the evening} (30:17), meaning the hour you enter the evening. And it is applied to forty years. Allah says, {Has there come upon man a period of time from the dahr} (76:1), meaning forty years. And it is applied to six months. Allah says, {It gives its fruit every hin} (14:25). Ibn 'Abbas said: the one given drink from it [is] every six months. So it is interpreted accordingly, because it is the middle, and the best of affairs is their middle, because the moment is not intended for prohibition by oath due to the ability to refrain without it, and forty years are like eternity, and one who hopes to live forty years. And al-zaman is used like al-hin. It is said: 'I have not seen you since a hin and since a zaman', and the definite and indefinite are equal, because six months, being customary, custom refers to it, and its start is considered from the time of the oath. So if he swore not to speak to him for al-zaman or al-hin or zamanan or hinan and did not intend, then [it is] six months. So if he spoke to him before the lapse of six months, he breaks the oath, and after it, he does not. According to al-Shafi'i: al-zaman and al-hin [are] an hour. According to Malik: a year. The dispute is when he has no intention, but if he intended something, then [it is] according to what he intended, because it can bear his speech. ('Ayni, Fath) Six [months] is the predicate of the subject, meaning: if he said, 'I will not speak to so-and-so al-zaman or al-hin or zamanan or hinan', it is upon six months from the time of his oath. (T, 'Ayni) And al-dahr: a subject, meaning: he said, 'I will not speak to so-and-so al-dahr' with the definite article. ('Ayni) And al-abad: even if he swore not to speak to so-and-so al-dahr or al-abad, the oath is valid upon the lifetime, because their use with the definite article is only for the lifetime. As for al-dahr, the poet said: 'Time passes and days, and sin remains, and the messenger of death comes while the heart is heedless.' Similarly al-abad, he (peace be upon him) said: 'Whoever fasts al-abad has no fast', meaning he fasted his entire lifetime. According to al-Shafi'i and Ahmad: it applies to an hour. According to Malik: a year.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof (consensus). It addresses an oath where the swearer says 'I will not speak to the owner of this taylasan (a specific garment).' If the owner sells the taylasan and the swearer later speaks to him, the oath is broken by consensus. The reasoning is that the prohibition (the oath) attaches to the person's essence (dhat), not to the garment. Thus, even if the garment is sold, the person remains the same. However, if the swearer speaks to the buyer of the garment, he does not break the oath because the owner is a different person. The text then discusses the terms 'zaman' and 'hin' (time) and their indefinite forms. The Hanafi position is that these terms, when used in oaths without specific intention, denote six months. This is derived from the middle interpretation of the word 'hin' in the Quran, which can mean hour, forty years, or six months. Six months is chosen as it is the moderate interpretation. The start is counted from the time of the oath. If the swearer speaks before six months, he breaks the oath; after six months, he does not. There is a disagreement with al-Shafi'i (who says it means an hour) and Malik (who says it means a year). The passage also covers 'dahr' and 'abad' (eternity). When used with the definite article, they mean the swearer's entire lifetime. The proof is from linguistic usage and a hadith. Again, Shafi'i and Ahmad say it means an hour, Malik says a year.

Key Terms
الطيلسان (al-taylasan): A specific garment, a round black cap worn over the turban, originally Persian.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man swears, 'I will not speak to the owner of this taylasan.' He sells the taylasan and then speaks to the former owner. What is the Hanafi ruling?
A) He does not break the oath because the garment is no longer his.
B) He breaks the oath because the prohibition is on the person's essence.
C) He breaks the oath only if he knew the garment was sold.
D) The oath is invalid because the object changed.

Key Takeaway
An oath attached to a person's essence remains binding even if the object changes.
