Four Scenarios That Waive Hadd for Theft
Ruling on four conditions that waive the hadd penalty

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four scenarios where the hadd penalty for theft is waived and explain the Hanafi reasoning based on shubha and the condition of nisab.

Core Idea
Hadd for theft is averted by shubha—four specific scenarios where the nisab condition is no longer certain at execution.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq identifies four critical circumstances in which even after a valid theft conviction, the hand-cutting penalty is not inflicted. These cases rest on the principle that the hadd is repelled by ambiguities (shubhat). The Hanafi school applies this principle more broadly than others, leading to differences, especially in the scenario of a drop in market value.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four scenarios
From the passage, list the four cases where the hadd is not applied. Note that they all occur after the judge's ruling but before the cutting.
The first three are: return to owner, thief ownership, claim of ownership. The fourth is decrease in value.
2
Understand the common element of shubha
Each scenario introduces uncertainty (shubha) about the legal entitlement to the stolen property. The hadd is repelled because certainty is required for its execution.
Shubha can arise from ownership ambiguity or loss of the nisab condition.
3
Analyze the fourth scenario and its ikhtilaf
Focus on the decrease in value due to market price. Identify the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. Note their proofs.
Abu Hanifa requires nisab at execution; Muhammad says at taking is enough.
4
Examine the Hanafi qiyas reasoning
The Hanafi proof uses analogy to the case where the thief consumes the entire item. In both consumption and market drop, the nisab is no longer physically present, but consumption is guaranteed (daman) while market drop is not. Hence, the hadd is waived only in the latter.
This distinguishes between decrease of the 'ayn (item) and decrease of qeema (value).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

عليه. (مستخلص، عيني) إلى مالكه أو ولده أو ذوي رحمه الذين في عياله. (ط) أو ملكه أي أو ملك السارق الشيء المسروق. (عيني) بعد القضاء بالقطع ولو كان الملك بهبة وتسليم. (ط) من النصاب بنقصان السعر في بلد الخصومة بعد القضاء قبل القطع (عيني، (ط) لم يقطع: جواب المسائل الأربعة، أي في جميعها. (عيني، ط)
The manner of cutting and its proof: If (the stolen item) returns to its owner or his possession after the judgment, or he claims it is his, or its value decreases from the nisab, he is not cut. And if two confess to theft then one says 'it is my property', they are not cut. And if two steal and one is absent, and they testify against them, the other is cut. And if a slave confesses to theft, he is cut. The fourth issue mentioned by his saying 'or decreased' means if he steals an item and its value decreases from the nisab in terms of price after judgment before cutting, he is also not cut. Muhammad said he is cut, and it is the saying of Zufar, al-Shafi'i, and Ahmad; due to the existence of nisab at the time of taking. Our proof: since nisab is a condition, its existence at the time of execution is a condition, unlike the decrease of the item itself as if he consumed it entirely; because it is guaranteed by the thief, but the decrease in price is not guaranteed by him.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage lists four scenarios where the hadd penalty of cutting the hand is not applied due to shubha (uncertainty). The first scenario: the stolen item returns to its owner or his possession (including to his child or dependent relative) after the judge's ruling. The second: the thief comes to own the item after judgment, even by gift and delivery. The third: the thief claims ownership of the item after the ruling but without proof. The fourth: the item's value decreases below the nisab due to a drop in market price after judgment but before the cutting is carried out. In this last case, Abu Hanifa holds that cutting is waived because the condition of nisab must persist at the time of execution. Muhammad, Zufar, al-Shafi'i, and Ahmad argue that the nisab at the time of taking suffices. The Hanafi response distinguishes between decrease in the item itself (which is guaranteed by the thief and thus does not affect the condition) and decrease in price (which is not guaranteed and thus breaks the condition). The passage also indicates that these four cases are the answer to the four issues, all of which prevent cutting.

Key Terms
النصاب (an-nisab): The minimum value of stolen property required for the hadd penalty to apply (10 dirhams or 1 dinar).
الشبهة (ash-shubha): Uncertainty or doubt about the legal entitlement that prevents the application of the hadd penalty.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a thief steals an item worth the nisab, but after the judge's ruling and before the cutting, the market price drops so that the item is now below the nisab, what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) The thief is cut because the nisab existed at the time of taking.
B) The thief is not cut because the nisab must exist at the time of execution.
C) The thief is cut only if the item itself decreased in value.
D) The thief is not cut only if he claims ownership.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the hadd penalty for theft is averted in four specific post-judgment scenarios due to shubha, with the condition of nisab at execution being key.

Two Thieves Confess, One Retracts: Shubha and the Dropping of Hadd
Ruling on two thieves confessing then one claims ownership

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling when one of two confessing thieves retracts and identify the disagreement with Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
When one thief retracts, shubha drops the hadd from both—even the one who did not retract.

This passage deals with a situation where two men confess to theft, but one later claims the stolen property is his. How does the Hanafi school handle the hadd penalty? The key concept is shubha (uncertainty) which prevents punishment. We'll explore the main Hanafi view and a divergent opinion from Abu Yusuf.

Guided Path
1
State the case
Identify the scenario: two men jointly confess to theft, then one claims the stolen item is his property. This retraction occurs before the hadd is executed.
The confession is joint, indicating partnership in the theft.
2
Apply the hadd principle
Theft hadd requires certainty. The retraction creates shubha (uncertainty) regarding the partnership. Because the hadd is dropped for the retractor, this shubha extends to the other, so the hadd is dropped from both.
The basis is that the theft is 'established on partnership,' and the retraction invalidates the partnership for hadd purposes.
3
Consider the exception
If only one confesses and names an accomplice who denies, the confessor is cut because no partnership is established. Here the confessor's own act remains, and the denial does not create shubha for him.
This contrast clarifies why shubha arises only when both originally confessed.
4
Compare Abu Yusuf's view
Abu Yusuf argues that since the joint confession is the basis, voiding the partnership through retraction nullifies the entire confession, so neither should be cut. He uses the analogy of qisas: if one says 'I killed with so-and-so' and the other denies, only the confessor is executed.
Abu Yusuf's analogy focuses on the act being shared; if the partnership is gone, the confession of a shared act cannot stand.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position (of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) is that the non-retractor's own confession remains valid, so without his own retraction, his hadd would normally apply. However, the retraction by the partner creates shubha, so the hadd is dropped from both. The difference from qisas is that in hadd, shubha is given more weight to avoid punishment.
The text says 'wa lahum' (their proof) for the majority, and this is the position upheld in fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو أقرا: أي أقر رجلان بسرقة ثم قال أحدهما: "إنّ المسروق مالي" يسقط القطع عنهما؛ لأن السرقة تثبت على الشركة، وبطل الحد عن أحدهما برجوعه، فأورث شبهة في حق الآخر، بخلاف ما لو قال: "سرقت أنا وفلان كذا" وفلان ينكر حيث يقطع المقر؛ لعدم الشركة بتكذيبه، وفيه خلاف أبي يوسف هو يقول: إنه أقر بفعل مشترك، وقد بطلت الشركة فلا تثبت ولهما أن الشركة لما لم تثبت بإنكار الآخر صار فعله كالعدم، وعدم فعله لا يخل بالموجود منه كقوله: قتلت أنا وفلان فلاناً، وقال الآخر: ما قتلت يقاد المقر وحده. رمز الحقائق: ٤١١] (مستخلص) هو مالي سواء أو ادعيا قبل القضاء، أو بعده قبل الإمضاء ( عيني)
And if two confess: i.e., two men confess to theft then one of them says 'the stolen item is my property', the cutting is dropped from both; because the theft is established on partnership, and the hadd is nullified for one by his retraction, so it creates shubha regarding the other. Unlike if he said 'I and so-and-so stole such-and-such' and the other denies, then the confessor is cut; because partnership is not proven due to the denial. There is a disagreement with Abu Yusuf who says: he confessed to a shared act, and the partnership has become void, so it is not established. Their proof: since partnership was not established by the other's denial, his act becomes like non-existence, and non-existence of his act does not harm what exists from him, like his saying 'I and so-and-so killed Zayd' and the other says 'I did not kill', then the confessor alone is subject to retaliation.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage discusses the case of two men who confess to theft, but then one of them claims that the stolen property actually belongs to him. The Hanafi ruling is that the hadd penalty is dropped from both of them. The reasoning is that the theft was established on a partnership between the two. When one retracts by claiming the property is his, the hadd is nullified for him due to his retraction, and this creates shubha (uncertainty) that prevents the hadd from being applied to the other as well. However, if one says 'I and so-and-so stole this' but the other denies any involvement, then the confessor alone is cut because there is no partnership established. Abu Yusuf disagrees: he holds that the confessor has admitted to a shared act, but since the partnership is voided by the retraction, the confession to the shared act cannot stand, so both should be spared. The Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) is that the confessor's own act remains, and the non-existence of the other's act does not negate it, analogous to the law of qisas where if one says 'I and so-and-so killed' and the other denies, the confessor is subject to retaliation.

Key Terms
الشبهة (ash-shubha): Uncertainty that prevents application of hadd, here arising from the partial retraction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Two men confess to stealing a purse. After their confession but before the judge's ruling, one says 'The purse is actually mine.' What is the Hanafi ruling?
A) Both are cut because they confessed.
B) Only the one who retracted is not cut; the other is cut.
C) Neither is cut because the retraction creates shubha.
D) Both are cut only if the owner does not appear.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, if two thieves confess and one retracts by claiming ownership, the hadd is dropped from both due to shubha.

Two Thieves, One Absent: The Ruling on the Present Thief
Ruling on cutting the present thief when the accomplice is absent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on cutting the present thief when the accomplice is absent and explain the rationale behind Abu Hanifa's later opinion.

Core Idea
A mere hypothetical shubha does not prevent the hadd; only an actual claim does.

This passage addresses a specific scenario in the law of theft (hadd al-sariqa). Two thieves commit theft together; one is captured, the other flees. Witnesses testify against both. The question is whether the captured thief can be punished with amputation (qat' al-yad) despite the absence of the accomplice. Abu Hanifa initially refrained from cutting, but later reversed his opinion. We will trace his reasoning and understand the principle of shubha in hadd punishments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Two thieves steal together. One is present, the other absent. Witnesses testify against both. Determine the legal question: should the present thief be cut?
2
State Abu Hanifa's two positions
Abu Hanifa initially said 'not cut' due to potential shubha, then later said 'cut'. Identify the earlier and later opinions.
This is an example of a mujtahid changing his view.
3
Analyse the earlier position
Why did he initially say not cut? Because if the absent thief were present, he might claim a shubha (e.g., ownership) that would prevent the hadd.
This is based on the principle that shubha averts hadd.
4
Analyse the later position
Why did he change? Because the absence of the accomplice prevents the theft from being established against him, so his potential claim is non-existent. A non-existent thing cannot create shubha. A mere hypothetical possibility is shubhat al-shubha and is disregarded.
Only an actual claim by a present person can create shubha.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The later opinion is the relied-upon position for fatwa. The present thief is cut immediately without waiting for the absent accomplice.
This is the operative ruling in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وغاب أحدهما : أي إذا سرق رجلان ثم غاب أحدهما، ثم شهد شاهدان على سرقتهما قطع الآخر في قول أبي حنيفة، وهو قوله آخراً، وكان يقول أولاً: لا يقطع؛ لأنه لو حضر ربما يدعي الشبهة عند حضوره، ثم رجع وقال: يقطع؛ لأن الغيبة تمنع ثبوت السرقة على الغائب فيبقى معدوما والمعدوم لا يورث الشبهة، فلا عبرة لتوهم حدوث الشبهة أي لو حضر وادعى كان شبهة، واحتمال الدعوى شبهة الشبهة فلا يعتبر. (مستخلص، فتح)
And if one of them is absent: i.e., if two men steal then one of them is absent, then two witnesses testify against them, the other is cut according to Abu Hanifa, and this is his later opinion. He used to say initially: he is not cut; because if he were present he might claim shubha at his presence. Then he retracted and said: he is cut; because absence prevents the establishment of theft against the absent one, so it remains non-existent, and the non-existent does not create shubha. So no consideration is given to the possibility of shubha occurring. That is, if he were present and claimed, it would be shubha, but the possibility of a claim is a shubha of shubha and thus not considered.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage addresses a scenario where two thieves commit theft but only one is present; the other is absent. Witnesses testify against both. Abu Hanifa originally held that the present thief should not be cut because if the absent thief were present, he might claim shubha (e.g., ownership) that would prevent the hadd. However, Abu Hanifa later changed his view and ruled that the present thief is cut. The reason is that the absence of the other thief prevents the theft from being established against him, so his potential claim is purely hypothetical. A mere possibility of shubha does not actualize shubha. Only if the absent thief were present and actually made a claim would it create shubha. The hypothetical potential for a claim is considered a 'shubha of shubha' and is disregarded.

Key Terms
شبهة الشبهة (shubhat ash-shubha): A mere possibility or hypothetical shubha that does not have actual legal effect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Two thieves steal together. One is captured, the other flees. Witnesses testify against both. According to Abu Hanifa's final opinion, what happens to the captured thief?
A) He is not cut because the absent thief might have a defense.
B) He is cut because the absent thief's potential claim does not create shubha.
C) He is not cut unless the absent thief is also caught.
D) He is cut only if he confesses.

Key Takeaway
Only an actual claim of shubha by a present accomplice can prevent the hadd; a mere hypothetical possibility is disregarded.

Slave's Confession of Theft: Cutting vs. Property Rights in Hanafi Law
Ruling on slave's confession with intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi positions on whether cutting or property is primary when a slave confesses to theft, and evaluate the practical consequence of each position.

Core Idea
A slave's confession to theft triggers a dispute: is cutting primary (Abu Hanifa), property primary (Muhammad), or both primary (Abu Yusuf)?

The source passage presents a detailed intra-Hanafi and inter-madhhab dispute regarding the legal effect of a slave's confession to theft. The central question is whether the hadd punishment (cutting the hand) and the restitution of stolen property are independent or one is subordinate to the other. This lesson will unpack the three main Hanafi positions and their practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal context
A slave confesses to theft. The slave may be authorized (ma'dhun) or interdicted (mahjur). The confession triggers two potential outcomes: cutting (hadd) and restitution of the stolen item.
The slave's capacity affects the validity of the confession in some opinions.
2
Examine the primary vs. subordinate dispute
The Hanafi masters differ on which legal effect is primary. Understand the logic of each: Abu Hanifa says cutting is primary, so property follows; Muhammad says property is primary, so cutting depends on it; Abu Yusuf says both are independent.
3
Trace the effect on the stolen property
If the master claims the stolen item as his own, who gets it? Under Abu Hanifa, the victim gets it; under Muhammad and Abu Yusuf, the master keeps it. This is the thamarah al-khilaf.
4
Note the external agreement and disagreement
Al-Shafi'i agrees with Abu Yusuf (both primary). Zufar distinguishes based on authorization, and Ahmad has his own view. This shows the inter-madhhab dimension.
5
Consider the Tahawi report
Al-Tahawi narrates that all three positions (cutting primary, property primary, both primary) are transmitted from Abu Hanifa himself, indicating internal development.
This highlights the richness of ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(مستخلص، فتح) ولو أقر عبد بسرقة قطع، مكلف مأذونا أو محجوراً عند أبي حنيفة أن هذا العين المسروقة مملوكة له بعد ما شهد الشاهدان بالسرقة عليه، لا يقطع أيضاً للشبهة، وقال الشافعي: يقطع ما لم تكن بينة حتى لا ينسد باب الحد. وحق القطع والمال، فتقطع يده ويرد المال على المسروق منه إن قائماً، كان هالكاً لا ضمان عليه، وتردّ السرقة إلى المسروق منه، ولا يجتمع قطع وضمان، أي قطع يد السارق صدقه مولاه أو كذبه، وإن كان محجوراً والمال هالك يقطع ولا يضمن كذبه مولاه أو صدقه، وإن كان قائماً وصدقه مولاه يقطع عندنا، ويرد المال على المسروق منه، وإن كذبه وقال المولى: المال مالي، قال أبو حنيفة: تقطع يده، والمال للمسروق منه؛ لأن المال تابع للقطع، وقال أبو يوسف والشافعي: تقطع يده والمال للمولى؛ لأن كلاً من القطع والمال أصل، وقال محمد: لا تقطع والمال للمولى؛ لأن المال عنده هو الأصل، وقال زفر: يصح إقراره بالمال إن كان مأذوناً، وإن كان محجوراً لا يصح إقراره بالمال، ولا يصح إقراره في حق القطع مأذوناً كان أو محجوراً؛ لأن إقراره بالقطع يتضرر به المولى، فلا يقبل إقراره عليه. قلنا: صحة إقراره عليه من حيث أنه آدمي ثم يتعدى إلى المالية في ضمنه فيصح، وحاصل هذا الخلاف راجع إلى أن المال أصل أو القطع أو كلاهما، فعند أبي حنيفة: القطع هو الأصل والمال تابع، وعند محمد: المال هو الأصل فلا يثبت القطع بدونه وعند أبي يوسف كلاهما أصل، وحكى الطحاوي أن الأقاويل الثلاثة مروية عن أبي حنيفة، فقوله الأول أخذ به محمد والثاني أخذ به أبو يوسف. (عيني، فتح)
And if a slave confesses to theft, he is cut, whether he is a responsible person, authorized or interdicted according to Abu Hanifa, that this specific stolen item is owned by him after the two witnesses testified to the theft against him, he is not cut also due to shubha. Al-Shafi'i said: He is cut as long as there is no clear proof, so that the door of punishment is not closed. The fourth issue mentioned by his saying 'or decreased' means if he steals an item and its value decreases from the nisab in terms of price after judgment before cutting, he is also not cut. Muhammad said he is cut, and it is the saying of Zufar, al-Shafi'i, and Ahmad; due to the existence of nisab at the time of taking. Our proof: since nisab is a condition, its existence at the time of execution is a condition, unlike the decrease of the item itself as if he consumed it entirely; because it is guaranteed by the thief, but the decrease in price is not guaranteed by him. (Mukhtasar, 'Ayni) To its owner or his child or his relatives who are in his household. (T) Or his ownership i.e. the thief owns the stolen item. ('Ayni) After judgment for cutting, even if the ownership is by gift and delivery. (T) From the nisab by decrease in price in the land of litigation after judgment before cutting. ('Ayni, T) He is not cut: the answer to the four issues, i.e. in all of them. ('Ayni, T) And if two confess: i.e. two men confess to theft then one of them says 'the stolen item is my property', the cutting is dropped from both... (repetition of earlier text) ... And the dispute over the slave: the truth of cutting and property, so his hand is cut and the money is returned to the victim if it still exists, if it is destroyed there is no guarantee, and the stolen item is returned to the victim, and cutting and guarantee do not combine. i.e. cutting the thief's hand whether his master believes him or denies him. If he is interdicted and the property is destroyed, he is cut and does not guarantee, whether his master denies him or believes him. If the property still exists and his master believes him, he is cut according to us, and the money is returned to the victim. If his master denies him and says the money is mine, Abu Hanifa said: his hand is cut and the money is for the victim; because the property is subordinate to the cutting. Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i said: his hand is cut and the money is for the master; because both cutting and property are primary. Muhammad said: he is not cut and the money is for the master; because in his view the property is primary. Zufar said: his confession regarding property is valid if he is authorized, but if interdicted it is not valid, and his confession regarding cutting is not valid whether authorized or interdicted, because his confession to cutting harms the master, so it is not accepted against him. We say: the validity of his confession against him is that he is a human being, then it extends to the property within it, so it is valid. The essence of this dispute reduces to whether the property is primary or the cutting or both. According to Abu Hanifa: cutting is primary and property is subordinate. According to Muhammad: property is primary so cutting is not established without it. According to Abu Yusuf: both are primary. Al-Tahawi narrated that all three sayings are transmitted from Abu Hanifa; his first saying was adopted by Muhammad and his second by Abu Yusuf. ('Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage, though containing some repeated elements, primarily deals with the legal status of a slave who confesses to theft. The Hanafi position is that the slave's hand is cut for the hadd, but there is significant internal and external disagreement regarding the disposition of the stolen property. The dispute hinges on whether the cutting (hadd) or the property right (mal) is considered the primary legal effect of the confession. Abu Hanifa holds that the cutting is primary, so once the slave is cut, the property is returned to the victim, regardless of the master's claim. Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i consider both cutting and property to be primary, so the slave is cut but the property goes to the master if he claims it. Muhammad holds that the property is primary; if the master claims the property, the cutting does not occur. Zufar makes a distinction based on whether the slave is authorized (ma'dhun) or interdicted (mahjur), and argues that the slave's confession does not affect the master's right. The Hanafi response, attributed to Abu Hanifa, is that the slave's confession is valid because he is a human being, and the property implication follows the cutting. Al-Tahawi notes that all three positions (cutting primary, property primary, both primary) are actually transmitted from Abu Hanifa himself, with Muhammad adopting the first and Abu Yusuf the second. The passage also reiterates the principle that cutting and financial guarantee (daman) do not combine in Hanafi law.

Key Terms
مأذون (ma'dhun): A slave who has been authorized by his master to engage in trade.
محجور (mahjur): A slave who is interdicted (not authorized) from trading or making binding statements.
قطع (qat'): Cutting of the hand as hadd punishment for theft.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference) between Abu Hanifa's and Muhammad's positions on a slave's confession to theft when the stolen item exists and the master claims it?
A) Under Abu Hanifa, the slave is cut and the property goes to the master; under Muhammad, the slave is not cut and the property goes to the victim.
B) Under Abu Hanifa, the slave is cut and the property goes to the victim; under Muhammad, the slave is not cut and the property goes to the master.
C) Under both, the slave is cut and the property goes to the victim.
D) Under Abu Hanifa, the slave is not cut; under Muhammad, the slave is cut.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's internal dispute over a slave's confession shows that when multiple legal effects are at play, priority may shift between hadd punishment and property rights.

Cutting and Guarantee Do Not Combine
Principle that hadd and financial liability are mutually exclusive

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that hadd and financial liability are mutually exclusive, cite the supporting hadith, explain the Hanafi response to objections, and distinguish between judicial ruling (qada') and religious duty (diyanatan).

Core Idea
Hadd and financial guarantee are mutually exclusive: the thief's hand is cut but no compensation is ordered in court.

This lesson explores a fundamental Hanafi principle regarding the intersection of criminal punishment and financial restitution. When a thief's hand is cut, does he still owe the value of the stolen property? The Hanafi school answers with a definitive 'no' – these two consequences do not combine. We will examine the primary proof (a hadith), the objections raised against it, the Hanafi counter-arguments, and a subtle intra-Hanafi distinction that has practical implications.

Guided Path
1
State the principle and its scope
Identify the exact ruling: cutting and guarantee do not combine absolutely, whether the stolen property is destroyed or consumed, and whether the thief is wealthy or poor.
The principle applies to all cases of theft where hadd is imposed.
2
Examine the primary proof (dalil)
Read the hadith 'La ghurm 'ala al-sariq ba'da qat'i yaminihi' and analyze its transmission and meaning. Note that the indefinite 'ghurm' in a negative context implies generality.
The hadith is the cornerstone of the Hanafi position.
3
Understand the objections and Hanafi responses
Review the criticisms of the hadith (weak chain, alternative interpretations) and how the Hanafis defend it: they reject the solitary criticism, consider mursal as proof, and uphold the general negation.
The defence uses usul principles like acceptance of mursal and the rule of indefinite in negation.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi nuance (diyanatan)
Imam Muhammad's view, as reported by Abu al-Layth, distinguishes between judicial enforcement (qada') and religious conscience (diyanatan). In court, no liability; but the thief morally ought to compensate.
This view is considered 'ahsanu' (better) and influences fatwa.
5
Compare with other schools
Contrast the Hanafi position with Shafi'i (always combine), Malik (combine if wealthy), and Zufar (always combine). Identify the thamarah al-khilaf.
The practical difference: a Hanafi judge does not order compensation; a Shafi'i judge does.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يجتمع قطع وضمان [ أي ضمان المال مطلقاً سواء هلك أو استهلك، وسواء كان السارق موسراً أو معسراً. (ع، مس] كالحد . مع العقر؛ لأن وجوب الضمان ينافي القطع؛ لأن السارق يتملك المسروق بأداء الضمان مستنداً إلى وقت الأخذ، فيتبين أنه ورد على ملكه فينبغي القطع، أي لا يجتمعان مطلقاً . يعني في الهلاك والاستهلاك واليسار والإعسار عندنا، وعند الشافعي: يجمع بينهما مطلقاً؛ لأنهما حقان مختلفان في السبب، فيستوفي كل منهما؛ لأن القطع حق الشرع وسببه ترك الانتهاء عما نهي عنه، والضمان حق العبد، وسببه أخذ المال، وبه قال أحمد وزفر، وعند مالك: يجمع بينهما إذا كان السارق موسراً وإن كان معسراً فلا ولنا: قوله عليه: "لا غرم على السارق بعد قطع يمينه"، أخرجه الدار قطني، رواه أبو حنيفة عن ابن مسعود له، و لم يرد عن غيره خلاف. فإن قلت الحديث ،معلول قال الدار قطني وفي إسناده سعيد بن إبراهيم، وهو مجهول، ويروى بطرق كلها لا تثبت، ولو سلم احتمل أنه أراد بنفي الغرم نفي العذاب في الآخرة، ويحتمل أنه أراد أجرة الحداد، ويحتمل أنه أراد نفي الضمان أبداً كما كان في بدء الإسلام، ومع هذه الاحتمالات لا يمكن الاحتجاج به لنفي الضمان؟ قلت: قول الدار قطني لا يقبل إذا انفرد، وإنما تكلموا في الحديث من حيث إسناده؛ لأنه رواه المسور عن عبد الرحمن بن عوف والمسور لم يلقه وهذا إن ثبت فهو صفة الإرسال والمرسل حجة، والنبي عليه ذكر الغرم منكراً في موضع النفي، والنكرة في موضع النفي تعم، فينتفي عنه جميع أنواع الغرم، وقال الفقيه أبو الليث: روي عن محمد ابن الحسن لله أنه قال: إنما لم يجب عليه الضمان في الحكم والقضاء، وأما ديانة فالضمان واجب، وهذا القول أحسن. [رمز الحقائق: ٤١١/١] (فتح)
And cutting and guarantee do not combine, i.e., guarantee of the property absolutely, whether it is destroyed or consumed, and whether the thief is wealthy or poor. (Ayn, Misk) Like the hadd with the fine; because obligation of guarantee contradicts cutting because the thief owns the stolen item by paying guarantee based on the time of taking, so it becomes clear that he came upon his own property, so cutting should not occur. That is, they do not combine absolutely, meaning in destruction, consumption, wealth, and poverty according to us. According to al-Shafi'i, they combine absolutely; because they are two different rights in cause, so each is fulfilled; because cutting is the right of the Shari'a and its cause is not refraining from what is forbidden, and guarantee is the right of the slave (i.e., the victim), and its cause is taking the property. Ahmad and Zufar also say this. According to Malik, they combine if the thief is wealthy, but if poor then not. Our proof: his saying (peace be upon him): 'There is no liability (ghurm) on the thief after his hand is cut.' Al-Daraqutni narrated it; Abu Hanifa narrated it from Ibn Mas'ud, and no one else opposed it. If you say: the hadith is defective, al-Daraqutni said that in its chain is Sa'id ibn Ibrahim, who is unknown, and it is narrated through paths all of which are not established. Even if accepted, it is possible that by negating ghurm he meant negating punishment in the afterlife, or it is possible he meant the fee of the executioner, or it is possible he meant negating guarantee forever as it was in the beginning of Islam. With these possibilities, it cannot be used as evidence to negate guarantee. I say: the statement of al-Daraqutni is not accepted if he is alone, and they only spoke about the hadith in terms of its chain; because al-Miswar narrated it from 'Abd al-Rahman ibn 'Awf, and al-Miswar did not meet him. If this is established, it is a characteristic of mursal, and mursal is a proof. The Prophet mentioned ghurm in the indefinite form in the context of negation, and the indefinite in the context of negation is general, so all types of ghurm are negated for him. Al-Faqih Abu al-Layth said: it is narrated from Muhammad ibn al-Hasan that he said: Indeed, guarantee is not obligatory upon him in terms of judiciary and ruling, but in religious terms (diyanatan) guarantee is obligatory. And this saying is best.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage establishes the Hanafi principle that the hadd penalty of cutting the hand and the financial liability (daman/ghurm) for the stolen property are mutually exclusive: the thief is not required to pay compensation after his hand is cut. This applies whether the item is destroyed or consumed, and whether the thief is wealthy or poor. The Hanafis base this on the hadith 'No ghurm upon the thief after his hand is cut.' The passage then addresses objections to the hadith's authenticity and interpretation. Al-Daraqutni considered the chain weak due to an unknown narrator and other issues. However, the Hanafi response defends the hadith: al-Daraqutni's solitary criticism is not accepted; the hadith is mursal but mursal is a valid proof in Hanafi usul. Moreover, the indefinite 'ghurm' in a negative context implies general negation of all financial liability. The passage also notes alternative interpretations (negation of afterlife punishment, or executioner's fee, or complete waiver in early Islam) but considers them weak. Finally, it cites al-Faqih Abu al-Layth who reports from Muhammad ibn al-Hasan (Imam Muhammad) that the non-liability is only in the judicial ruling (qada'), but in religious conscience (diyanatan), the thief should still make amends. This latter view is considered preferable by some. The opposition includes al-Shafi'i, Ahmad, and Zufar who hold that cutting and guarantee can combine because they are separate rights (public and private). Malik makes a distinction based on the thief's financial status.

Key Terms
الغرم (al-ghurm): Financial liability or compensation for the stolen property.
ديانة (diyanatan): In terms of religious obligation, as opposed to qada'an (judicial enforcement).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling if a thief's hand is cut but the stolen item has already been consumed?
A) The thief must pay the value of the item.
B) The thief does not pay anything in court, but it is religiously recommended to compensate.
C) The thief must pay only if he is wealthy.
D) The thief must pay and also be imprisoned.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, hadd and financial liability never judicially combine, but the thief remains morally obliged to compensate the victim.

Theft of a Sheep and Slaughter Within the Hirz: No Hadd
Ruling on theft of a sheep slaughtered within the enclosure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between theft of a whole animal and theft of its meat, and determine when the hadd penalty is applied in Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
Slaughtering the sheep inside the enclosure changes the stolen property to meat, removing the hadd penalty.

In Hanafi law, the application of the hadd penalty for theft depends on what is stolen and from where. A key nuance arises when the thief alters the property before removing it from the secure place. This lesson examines the ruling on a thief who steals a sheep, slaughters it inside the enclosure, and then takes out the meat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its location
The thief steals a sheep from the hirz (enclosure), slaughters it inside, then removes the meat. The key is the sequence: slaughter before removal.
The ruling distinguishes between alteration inside vs. outside the hirz.
2
Determine the legal object of theft
The theft is considered complete on the meat, not the whole sheep, because the slaughter changes the item's legal identity.
In Hanafi fiqh, no hadd for meat even if valuable.
3
Apply the rule: no hadd for meat
Since the theft was completed on meat, the hadd penalty does not apply. The thief is only liable for the value.
The value of the meat may be less than the whole sheep, but that does not affect the ruling.
4
Compare with the opposite scenario
If the thief removes the sheep alive from the hirz, then slaughters outside, the hadd applies because the theft was completed on the whole sheep while it was still intact.
The subsequent slaughter is a decrease in substance that is the thief's own responsibility.
5
Understand the underlying rationale
The decrease in substance (slaughter) is guaranteed against the thief, so it does not nullify the hadd if it occurs after removal. But if it occurs before removal, it changes the stolen item.
Contrast with decrease in price, which is not guaranteed and can drop the hadd.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو سرق شاةً فذبحها فأخرجها لا. أي من سرق شاة فذبحها في الدار ثم أخرجها منها لا يقطع وإن كانت قيمتها مذبوحة عشرة؛ لأن السرقة تمت على اللحم، ولا قطع فيه، ومعلوم أنه يضمن قيمتها، وإنما قيد بقوله: "فذبحها"؛ لأنه لو أخرجها حية من الحرز، وقيمتها عشرة، ثم ذبحها يقطع وإن انتقصت قيمتها بالذبح وهذا النقصان نقصان عين بخلاف النقصان السابق بعد القضاء قبل الاستيفاء، فإنه نقصان سعر، ولهذا أوجب سقوط القطع هناك؛ لأن نقصان السعر غير مضمون على السارق، أما هنا فلا يوجب سقوط القطع؛ لكون نقصان العين مضموناً عليه.
And if he steals a sheep and slaughters it and takes it out: no. That is, whoever steals a sheep and slaughters it in the enclosure then takes it out of it, he is not cut, even if its value slaughtered is ten, because the theft was completed on the meat, and there is no cutting in it, and it is known that he is liable for its value. He only restricted it with his saying "so he slaughters it" because if he had taken it out alive from the guarded place and its value is ten, then he slaughters it, he is cut even if its value decreases by slaughter, and this decrease is a decrease in the substance, unlike the previous decrease after the court order before execution, for it is a decrease in price, and therefore it necessitated the dropping of cutting there because the decrease in price is not guaranteed against the thief, but here it does not necessitate dropping the cutting because the decrease in substance is guaranteed against him.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This is a ruling statement from the matn: if a thief steals a sheep, slaughters it inside the enclosure, and then takes it out, the amputation penalty (hadd) is not applied. The commentator explains the reasoning: the act of slaughter changes the stolen item from the whole sheep (which is subject to cutting if its value reaches the nisab) to meat, and there is no cutting for meat even if it is valuable. The thief is still liable to return the value. The qualification "fa-dhabahaha" (so he slaughters it) is crucial: if the thief had removed the sheep alive from the guarded place and then slaughtered it outside, the cutting would be applied because the theft was completed on the whole animal while it was still intact, and the subsequent slaughter is a decrease in substance that is the thief's own responsibility, so it does not nullify the cutting. The commentator distinguishes between a decrease in substance (nawqan 'ayn) and a decrease in price (nawqan si'r): the former is on the thief's guarantee, the latter is not, hence different rulings.

Key Terms
الحرز (al-hirz): The secured place from which theft is considered; here, the enclosure where the sheep is kept.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a thief steals a sheep worth 10 dirhams from a locked pen, slaughters it inside, and takes the meat out, what is the ruling?
A) The thief is subject to amputation.
B) The thief is not subject to amputation but must pay the value.
C) The thief is not subject to amputation and does not pay anything.
D) The thief is subject to amputation only if the meat is worth 10 dirhams.

Key Takeaway
Slaughtering the stolen sheep inside the hirz changes the stolen item to meat, thereby removing the hadd penalty, but the thief remains liable for the value.

Rulings on Jihad During a General Call and on Taking Compensation
Ruling on jihād becoming fard 'ayn and on taking compensation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which jihad becomes fard 'ayn in the Hanafi school and explain the ruling on taking compensation (ja'l) for equipping the army.

Core Idea
When the enemy attacks and the call is general, jihad becomes a personal duty overriding others' rights, and compensation from the people is allowed only if the treasury is empty.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq addresses two interconnected rulings of jihad: the moment it becomes fard 'ayn on every individual, and the permissibility of collecting funds from the community to support the army. Understanding these rulings requires distinguishing between ordinary jihad (fard kifayah) and the general call (nafir 'amm), as well as the source of public funds. The commentary expands on the conditions for women, slaves, debtors, and children, and provides evidence from the Prophet's and 'Umar's practices.

Guided Path
1
Identify the trigger for fard 'ayn
Locate the condition in the text that makes jihad a personal obligation: 'if the enemy attacks suddenly and the general call becomes widespread' (nafir 'amm). Without this trigger, jihad remains fard kifayah.
Note the distinction between ūridinary jihad and this emergency situation.
2
Analyse the implications for women and slaves
Understand that when jihad becomes fard 'ayn, women and slaves may go out without permission from husband or master, because individual obligations (furuḍ al-a'yān) override others' rights. Note the exception for women: a mahram is recommended; if none, the obligation remains but with restriction.
The commentary connects this to the ruling on Hajj, which is also fard 'ayn and requires a mahram.
3
Apply the condition of the treasury to the ruling on ja'l
Examine the ruling on taking compensation (ja'l) from people to equip the army. The text says it is disliked (makruh) if there is fay' in the treasury (wealth taken without fighting, like kharaj and jizya). If the treasury is empty, it is not disliked.
The evidence for permissibility when treasury is empty includes the Prophet taking armor from Safwan without consent in dire need, and 'Umar's practice.
4
Evaluate the ikhtilaf and the mufta bihi position
Note that some Hanafi scholars considered ja'l always disliked, but the commentator states 'the correct is the first position' – that it is not disliked when the treasury has no fay'. Understand that the mufta bihi is the permissive view during necessity.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf resolved by later scholars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن هجم العدو، فتخرج المرأة والعبد بلا إذن زوجها وسيده، وكره الجعل إن وجد فيء، وإلا لا. إن هجم العدو: أي أتى بغتة على بلادنا وصار النفير عاماً ولا يتهيأ دفعهم إلا بقتالهم جميعاً فيجب حينئذ على جميع الناس الدفع. فتخرج المرأة والعبد : متفرع على كون الجهاد فرض عين على كل الناس إذا غلب العدو كافراً كان أو باغياً، وينبغي أن يقيد الوجوب في المرأة بما إذا كان لها محرم يذهب معها للجهاد بدلیل اشتراط المحرم لها في الحج وهو فرض عين، وقوله: "بلا إذن زوجها وسيده؛ لأن حقهما لا يظهر في فروض الأعيان كما في الصلاة والصوم، بخلاف ما قبل النفير ؛ لأن بغيرهما يقام الفرض، فلا حاجة إلى إبطال حق المولى والزوج، وكذا المديون والولد بلا إذن رب الدين والوالد يخرجان ويأثم الزوج ونحوه بالمنع. ولو كان للابن أبوان وأذن أحدهما دون الآخر، لا ينبغي له الخروج، ولهذا لما أراد العباس ابن مرداس الله الجهاد قال علي: "إلزم أمك فإن الجنة عند رجل أمك"، وهذا في غير النفير العام، وكذا كل سفر فيه خطر، وإن لم يكن فيه خطر فلا بأس بأن يخرج بغير إذنهما إذا لم يضيعهما، والأجداد أبو الأب والجدات أم الأم مثلهما عند عدمهما. (مستخلص فتح عيني) بلا إذن زوجها فيه لف ونشر مرتب؛ لأن الفروض المتعينة مقدمة على حق المولى والزوج كصلاة الفرض. (عيني، مستخلص) وكره الجعل إلخ الجعل بالضم ما يجعل للعامل على عمله والمراد ههنا أخذ المال من الناس لأجل الغزاة به أي إذا كان في بيت المال فيء وهو المال المأخوذ بلا قتال كالخراج والجزية، واحترز به عن مال الغنيمة والأموال الضائعة؛ فإنهما لا يصرفان للمقاتلة، لا يجعل الإمام على أرباب المال شيئاً لتجهيز الجيش من غير طيب أنفسهم؛ لأنه يشبه الأجر على الطاعة، فحقيقته حرام فيكره ما أشبهه، وقوله: "وإلا لا" أي وإن لم يوجد في بيت المال فيء لا يكره الجعل؛ لأن بيت المال معد لفوائد المسلمين فإذا لم يكن فيه فيء فلا بأس بأن يقوي بعضهم بعضاً؛ لأن فيه دفع الضرر الأعلى بإلحاق الأدنى ولأن الحاجة إلى الجهاد ماسة، وقد أخذ النبي الله دروعاً من صفوان عند الحاجة بغير رضاه، وعمر الله كان يغزي العزب عن ذي الحليلة، أي يبعث من لا زوجة له عوض من له زوجة، ويعطي الشاخص أي الذاهب فرس القاعد، وقيل: يكره أيضاً، والصحيح الأول.
If the enemy attacks, then a woman and a slave go out without permission of her husband and his master, and it is disliked to take compensation (ja'l) if there is fay' (in the treasury), otherwise not. Explanation of the attack: meaning it comes suddenly upon our land and the general call (nafir) becomes widespread, and repelling them is not possible except by all fighting, so then it becomes obligatory upon all people to repel. The woman and slave go out: this is derived from jihad becoming fard 'ayn upon every person when the enemy overwhelms, whether disbeliever or rebel. It should be restricted for women to when she has a mahram to go with her for jihad, due to the condition of a mahram for her in Hajj which is fard 'ayn. The statement: 'without permission of her husband or his master' because their rights do not appear in individual obligations (furuḍ al-a'yān) as in prayer and fasting, unlike before the general call, because without them the obligation is fulfilled by others, so there is no need to nullify the right of the master and husband. Similarly, the debtor and the child go out without permission of the creditor and parent, and the husband and the like sin by preventing. If the son has two parents and one gives permission and the other not, it is not appropriate for him to go out. For this, when al-'Abbas ibn Mirdas intended jihad, 'Ali said: 'Adhere to your mother, for Paradise is at the feet of your mother.' This is outside the general call. Similarly for any journey with danger, but if there is no danger, there is no harm in going out without their permission if he does not neglect them. Grandfathers and grandmothers are like them in their absence. The dislike of compensation: 'ja'l' is what is given to a worker for his work, and here meaning taking money from people for the sake of the fighters, i.e., if there is fay' in the treasury, which is wealth taken without fighting like kharaj and jizya. He excludes booty and lost wealth, for they are not spent on fighters. The imam may not impose anything upon the wealthy for equipping the army without their consent, because it resembles a wage for obedience, so in essence it is forbidden and what resembles it is disliked. His statement: 'otherwise no', i.e., if there is no fay' in the treasury, taking compensation is not disliked, because the treasury is prepared for the benefits of Muslims, so if there is no fay', there is no harm in some strengthening others, because it repels the greater harm by incurring the lesser, and because the need for jihad is pressing. The Prophet took armor from Safwan at the time of need without his consent, and 'Umar used to send a bachelor in place of a married man, and give the horse of the stay-at-home to the one going. It was also said it is disliked, but the correct position is the first.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof (hukm with dalil) from the matn of Kanz al-Daqaiq on the laws of jihad. It covers two main rulings: first, when the enemy attacks and becomes a general call (nafir 'amm), jihad becomes fard 'ayn on every capable person, including women and slaves, who may then go out without permission from their husband or master because individual obligations override their rights. The commentary elaborates on conditions: a woman needs a mahram; a debtor or child also may go out without permission, but a parent's or creditor's objection may cause sin if not a general call. Second, the ruling on taking compensation (ja'l) from people to equip the army: if the treasury has fay' (non-booty wealth like kharaj), it is disliked to take such contributions from people without their consent, as it resembles a wage for obedience. If there is no fay', it is not disliked, based on necessity and precedent of the Prophet and 'Umar. The text follows the order of the matn, first explaining the conditional ruling, then the exceptions and proofs.

Key Terms
النفير العام (al-nafir al-‘āmm): The general call to arms when the enemy attacks, making jihad obligatory upon all.
الجعل (al-ja‘l): Compensation or payment given to someone for their work; here specifically money collected from people to equip the army.
الفيء (al-fay’): Wealth taken from non-Muslims without fighting, such as kharaj and jizya, which belongs to the public treasury.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When does jihad become fard 'ayn according to the Hanafi school?
A) When the imam declares war
B) When the enemy attacks unexpectedly and the general call (nafir 'amm) is made
C) Every year, like Hajj
D) Only when the Prophet's tomb is threatened

Key Takeaway
Jihad becomes a personal duty (fard 'ayn) only when the enemy attacks suddenly and the general call is made; at that point, obligations towards others are superseded, and the state may collect funds from the people if the treasury is empty.

Using Booty Items Before Division in Dar al-Harb
Ruling on using booty before division with conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which booty items may be used before division in dar al-harb and explain why the ruling changes after leaving dar al-harb.

Core Idea
Booty items may be used before division only in dar al-harb due to necessity; after leaving, use becomes haram.

This lesson examines a specific ruling from Kanz al-Daqaiq regarding the use of consumable booty items (like oil) while still in enemy territory. The Hanafi school permits such use out of necessity, but only as long as the army remains in dar al-harb. Understanding the rationale of necessity (darurah) and the role of the imam’s permission is essential to applying this rule correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize that the act is using consumable booty items (e.g., oil) before formal division. The ruling is permissible (mubah) while in dar al-harb, provided conditions are met.
Condition: imam does not forbid it.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is istihsan based on necessity (darurah). There is no direct textual evidence; the ruling is derived from the principle that necessity permits normally prohibited actions.
The dalil is rational, not textual.
3
Trace the condition of imam’s permission
The permission to use items is contingent on the imam not forbidding it. If the imam prohibits, it becomes impermissible.
The imam has authority over booty distribution.
4
Note the change after leaving dar al-harb
Once the army exits dar al-harb, the necessity ceases, and the items become joint property of the soldiers. Use becomes prohibited (haram) without explicit permission.
Leftover items must be returned to the booty pool.
5
Evaluate the consequence of leftover
Any unused portion must be returned to the general booty before division. After division, if the user is wealthy, he must give it in charity; if poor, he may keep it as luqatah.
This ensures fairness among soldiers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الغنائم وقسمتها.
ودهن بلا قسمة ولا يبيعها وبعد الخروج منها لا، وما فضل ردّ إلى الغنيمة.
وده بفتح الدال أي دهن أبدانهم أو حوافر دوابهم للتقليب. (عيني) بلا قسمة متعلق بـ"ينتفع"، أي ينتفع بهذه الأشياء بلا قسمة بينهم، وهذا إذا لم ينههم الإمام، فإن نهاهم لم يجز لهم ذلك. (عيني) ولا يبيعها: أي هذه الأشياء المذكورة التي يباح الانتفاع بها قبل القسمة؛ لأنه لا ملك له، وإنما أبيح له التناول للضرورة، فإن باع أحد قبل القسمة رد الثمن إلى الغنيمة؛ لأنه بدل عين كان للجماعة، وقوله : وبعد الخروج منها لا" أي لا يجوز الانتفاع بهذه الأشياء بعد الخروج من دار الحرب؛ لزوال المبيح وهو الضرورة، ولأنها صارت للغانمين، ولا يجوز أخذ المال المشترك إلا بإذن الشريك الآخر، ولأن الحكم دائر على دليل الحاجة، وهو كونهم في دار الحرب، فإذا لم يبق دليل الحاجة انتفت الحاجة. (مستخلص، فتح، عيني) لا: أي لا ينتفعون بالأشياء المذكورة لزوال الضرورة. (عيني) وما فضل: أي الذي بقي في يده من العلف ونحوه الأشياء المباحة. (عيني) رد: بعد الخروج إلى دار الإسلام لزوال حاجتهم. (عيني) إلى الغنيمة: لأنه حق الغانمين فلا يبقى الانتفاع به، وهذا قبل القسمة، أما بعدها فإن كان غنياً وكانت العين قائمة تصدق بها أو بقيمتها لو هالكة وإن كان فقيراً انتفع بها ولا شيء عليه إن هلك؛ لأنه لما تعذر الرد صار في حكم اللقطة. رمز الحقائق: ٤٢٤/١] (فتح)
Chapter of booty and its division. And oil without division, and he does not sell it, and after leaving it no, and what remains is returned to the booty. Oil (with fatha on dal) meaning oil of their bodies or hooves of their animals for anointing. (Ayni) 'Without division' modifies 'benefits', i.e., he benefits from these things without division among them, and this is if the imam does not forbid them; if he forbids them, they are not permitted. (Ayni) 'And he does not sell it': i.e., these mentioned things which it is permissible to benefit from before division because he has no ownership; only consumption is permitted for necessity; if one sells before division, the price is returned to the booty because it is a substitute for an asset belonging to the group. And his saying: 'and after leaving it, no' i.e., it is not permissible to benefit from these things after leaving dar al-harb because the permitter (necessity) ceases, and because they become property of the gains, and it is not permissible to take common property except with the permission of the other partner, and because the ruling revolves around the evidence of need, which is their being in dar al-harb; when the evidence of need no longer exists, the need ceases. (Mukhtasar, Fath, Ayni) 'No': i.e., they do not benefit from the mentioned things due to the cessation of necessity. (Ayni) 'And what remains': i.e., what remains in his hand of fodder and similar permissible things. (Ayni) 'Is returned': after leaving to dar al-islam due to cessation of their need. (Ayni) 'To the booty': because it is the right of the gains, so he cannot continue to benefit from it, and this is before division; after division, if he is rich and the asset still exists, he gives it in charity or its value if destroyed; if he is poor, he may benefit and nothing is due upon him if it perishes because when return becomes impossible, it becomes like luqatah. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1/424) (Fath)
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq establishes the ruling on using certain items from the booty (ghanimah) while still in dar al-harb, before formal division. The author states that oil (and similar consumables like medicines, perfume, violet oil) may be used without division, but selling them is prohibited. After leaving dar al-harb, such use is forbidden. Any leftover must be returned to the booty pool. The commentary elaborates: 'oil' here means oil for anointing bodies or horses' hooves, as a necessity. The permission applies only if the imam does not forbid it; if he does, it becomes impermissible. Sale before division is invalid because the soldier has no ownership; the proceeds must be returned. The rationale for the post-exit prohibition is that the necessity (being in enemy territory) that permitted the use has ceased, and the items have become joint property. The leftover rule: before division, any unused portion must be returned; after division, if the user is wealthy, he must give it in charity (or its value) as compensation; if poor, he may keep it—this is because return is impossible, and it becomes analogous to luqatah (lost property). The hadith cited in the convert section is not present here; the proof is rational (istihsan based on necessity).

Key Terms
دهن (duhn): Oil used for anointing bodies or horses' hooves; an example of consumable items.
الضرورة (al-darurah): Necessity that permits normally prohibited actions, here being in enemy territory.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is it prohibited to use booty items after leaving dar al-harb?
A) Because the imam forbids it
B) Because the items become property of the entire group and the necessity ceases
C) Because the soldier has no ownership
D) Because the items are haram

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi permission to use booty items before division is conditional upon being in dar al-harb and not being forbidden by the imam; after exit, the necessity ceases and use becomes haram.

Muslim Trader in Dar al-Harb: Property and Sadaqah
Ruling on Muslim trader entering Dar al-Harb with aman

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a Muslim trader who takes property from harbis under aman and explain why he must give sadaqah.

Core Idea
Taking property under aman is ownership but forbidden; sadaqah purifies the foul acquisition.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq deals with a Muslim trader who enters enemy territory (Dar al-Harb) with a pledge of safety (aman). The general rule is that he must not harm any harbi. However, if the enemy breaks the pact, he may retaliate. The specific focus here is: if the trader manages to bring property from the enemy to Dar al-Islam, does he own it? What must he do with it? The Hanafi school answers with a nuanced distinction between valid ownership and moral prohibition, requiring sadaqah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act
The act is a Muslim trader under aman taking property from harbis and bringing it to Dar al-Islam.
The aman implies a guarantee of safety; taking property without permission is a breach.
2
State the ruling
The trader owns the property (milk mahzur) but must give it as sadaqah.
Ownership is valid due to seizure of mubah property, but it is foul due to treachery.
3
Locate the dalil
The hadith: 'Every traitor will have a banner planted at his anus on the Day of Judgment.' This condemns treachery (ghadr), supporting the prohibition of benefiting from the taken property.
The hadith is ahad, zanni al-thubut, but sufficient for establishing the prohibition.
4
Understand the exception
If the enemy king or his agents break the aman first (e.g., by seizing Muslim property), the trader may retaliate and take their property as ghanimah.
Exception applies only when the breach is initiated by the harbis with the ruler's knowledge or permission.
5
Apply the reasoning
The sadaqah is required because the ownership is foul (khubth) due to the means of acquisition; it is analogous to a corrupt sale where the cause of ownership exists but the property is tainted.
The condition 'taking out' (ikhraj) is necessary; if the property is not brought out, repentance and return are required.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب المستأمن أي في بيان أحكامه دخل تاجرنا دار الحرب حرم تعرضه لشيء منهم فلو أخرج شيئاً ملكه محظوراً فيتصدق به قال عليه السلام لكل غادر لواء يغرز عند باب إسته يوم القيامة يعرف به غدره إلا إذا غدر ملكهم بأخذ الأموال أو الجنس أو غيره أو فعل ذلك غير الملك بعلم الملك ولم يمنعه فحينئذ يباح له التعرض فيجوز له أخذ أموالهم وقتل نفوسهم لأنهم هم الذين نقضوا العهد وليس له أن يستبيح فروجهم فإن الفروج لا تحل إلا بالملك ولا ملك قبل الإحراز بالدار إلا إذا وجد امرأته المأسورة أو أم ولده أو مدبرته ولم يطأهن أهل الحرب لأنهم لا يملكونها بالاستيلاء غير أنهم إذا وطؤوها يكون شبهة في حقهن فيجب عليهن العدة فلا يجوز له أن يطأهن حتى تنقضي عدتهن بخلاف أمته المأسورة حيث لا يجوز له أن يطأها وإن لم يطأها الحربي لأنهم ملكوها فصارت من جملة أموالهم فلو أخرج شيئاً أي لو أخرج التاجر شيئاً من أموالهم وأنفسهم إلينا ملكه ملكاً خبيثاً فيتصدق به أما ملكه فلورود الاستيلاء على مال مباح وأما كونه محظوراً فلأنه حصل بسبب الغدر فأوجب ذلك خبثاً فيه فيؤمر بالتصدق به وهذا لأن الحظر لغيره لا يمنع انعقاد سبب الملك كالبيع الفاسد وقيد بالإخراج لأنه إذا غصب شيئاً في دار الحرب ولم يخرجه وجب عليه التوبة وهي لا تحصل إلا بالرد عليهم فأشبه المشتري شراء فاسداً
Chapter of al-Musta'min, i.e. explanation of its rulings. Our trader entered Dar al-Harb; it is prohibited for him to harm any of them. If he takes out something, he owns it as a prohibited ownership, so he must give sadaqah with it. He (peace be upon him) said: 'Every traitor will have a banner planted at his anus on the Day of Judgment by which his treachery will be known.' Except if their king treacherously takes property or jizyah or other, or if someone other than the king does that with the king's knowledge and he does not prevent them, then at that time it becomes permissible for him to harm them. So it is permissible for him to take their property and kill their lives, because they broke the covenant. But he is not allowed to make their private parts permissible, because private parts are only permissible by ownership, and no ownership exists before taking possession in the land, except if he finds his captive wife or his umm walad or his mudabbara and the harbis have not had intercourse with them, because they do not own them by conquest. However, if they have had intercourse with them, it becomes a doubt concerning them, so they must observe iddah, and he cannot have intercourse with them until their iddah expires, contrary to his captured slave-girl where he cannot have intercourse with her even if the harbi did not have intercourse with her, because they owned her so she became part of their property. So if he takes out something, i.e. if the trader takes out something of their property or themselves to us, he owns it as a foul ownership, and he must give sadaqah with it. As for his ownership, it is due to the occurrence of seizure of permissible property; as for it being prohibited, it is because it was obtained through treachery, so that causes foulness in it, so he is ordered to give sadaqah with it. This is because prohibition for an external reason does not prevent the occurrence of the cause of ownership, like a corrupt sale. It is restricted to taking out, because if he seizes something in Dar al-Harb and does not take it out, he must repent, and repentance is only achieved by returning it to them, so it is similar to a buyer in a corrupt purchase.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the hadith on treachery. It first states the general rule: a Muslim trader entering Dar al-Harb with aman (safe conduct) is prohibited from harming any of the harbis in their lives or property, because the aman implies a guarantee of safety. To harm them would be treachery (ghadr), which is haram. The proof is the hadith: 'Every traitor will have a banner planted at his anus on the Day of Judgment by which his treachery will be known.' An exception is made if the enemy king or someone acting with his knowledge and consent breaks the aman first—then it becomes permissible for the Muslim to take their property and kill them, but not to take their women as captives for intercourse, because that requires full ownership which is not established until they are brought into Dar al-Islam (ihraz). Special rules apply to women already related to him (like his wife or slave-girl) if they are captured; if the harbis have not had intercourse with them, he can take them back without waiting; if they have, iddah is required. The passage then addresses the case where the trader manages to take property from them and bring it to Dar al-Islam. He owns it—because seizure of permissible property (mubah) is a cause of ownership—but the ownership is 'foul' (khubth) because it was obtained through treachery. Therefore he is not allowed to keep it; he must give it away as sadaqah. This is similar to a corrupt sale where the cause of ownership exists but the property is legally impaired. However, if he seizes property in Dar al-Harb but does not bring it out, he does not own it; rather he must repent and return it, because taking it out is a condition for ownership, similar to how a buyer in a corrupt sale must return the item if he hasn't taken possession.

Key Terms
مستأمن (musta'min): One who enters enemy territory with a pledge of safety (aman), whether a Muslim or a harbi.
غدر (ghadr): Betrayal or breach of a pledge, specifically the aman given to the enemy.
ملك محظور (milk mahzur): Ownership that is legally valid in cause but prohibited in effect due to the means of acquisition (e.g., through treachery).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What must a Muslim trader do with property he takes from harbis while under aman and brings to Dar al-Islam?
A) Keep it as war booty
B) Return it to the harbis
C) Give it as sadaqah
D) Destroy it

Key Takeaway
A Muslim under aman must honor the pledge; property taken in breach is owned but foul, requiring sadaqah to purify.

Debt and Usurpation Between Harbi and Musta'min
Ruling on adjudicating debts and usurpations from Dar al-Harb

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on adjudicating past transactions from Dar al-Harb and explain the role of wilaya (judicial jurisdiction) in determining enforceability.

Core Idea
Islamic courts judge only acts committed under their jurisdiction; past acts in Dar al-Harb are not adjudicable.

This passage from the chapter on al-musta'min (one entering Dar al-Islam with safe-conduct) clarifies the limits of judicial authority over transactions that occurred in Dar al-Harb. The key principle is that judgment requires valid jurisdiction (wilaya) both at the time of the act and at the time of judgment. Since the parties were not under Islamic law when the debt or usurpation occurred, the qadi cannot intervene. However, if they later become Muslims, the debt becomes enforceable, but not the usurpation, because the property taken was not considered inviolable at the time.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
Determine what transaction (debt or usurpation) occurred, between whom (harbi and Muslim, or two harbis), and in which territory (Dar al-Harb). The ruling states that nothing is adjudged when they later enter Dar al-Islam as musta'min, unless they become Muslims, in which case the debt (but not the usurpation) is adjudged.
Focus on the distinction between debt (valid by mutual consent) and usurpation (taking without right of inviolable property).
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The text provides a rational proof: 'because judgment depends on jurisdiction, and we had no jurisdiction over the musta'min at the time of the transaction, nor at the time of judgment.' This is a dalil aqli (rational argument) establishing wilaya as a necessary condition.
The proof is not from a textual source (Quran/Hadith) but from the nature of judicial authority.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The link between the ruling (no judgment) and the proof (lack of jurisdiction) is direct: without wilaya, the qadi has no authority to enforce. For the debt of a harbi who converts, jurisdiction now exists for future acts, but the past debt is considered a valid obligation that can be enforced on the Muslim party. For usurpation, the property was not ma'sum (inviolable), so no right attached to it that the qadi can restore.
Note that the validity of the debt as a religious obligation is separate from its enforceability in court.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf holds that the debt is adjudged against the Muslim party even if the other is a harbi, because the Muslim is subject to Islamic law and the debt is a right of a Muslim. The text's position (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) is that no judgment is given because both parties were not under jurisdiction at the time.
This difference stems from whether jurisdiction over the Muslim at the time of judgment is sufficient, even if the other party was not under jurisdiction at the time of the transaction.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: no judgment for past acts unless both become Muslims. Abu Yusuf's view is considered a minority within the school. The practical consequence is that a Muslim cannot be compelled by a qadi to pay a debt incurred with a harbi in Dar al-Harb, but he is advised religiously to discharge it.
The tarjih is based on the principle that wilaya at the time of the transaction is necessary; this is the more consistent application of the school's usul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب المستأمن: حربياً، أو غصب أحدهما صاحبه وخرجا إلينا، لم يقض بشيء، وكذا لو كانا حربيين فعلا ذلك ثم استأمنا، وإن خرجا مسلمين قضي بالدين بينهما، لا بالغصب. فلأن القضاء يعتمد الولاية، ولا ولاية لنا على المستأمن وقت الإدانة أصلاً، ولا وقت القضاء؛ لأنه ما التزم حكم الإسلام فيما مضى من أفعاله، وإنما التزم ذلك في المستقبل، وأما في الغصب فلا يقضى أيضاً بشيء لأحدهما؛ لأنه للذي غصبه واستولى عليه؛ لمصادفته مالاً غير معصوم ولكن يفتي المسلم بردّ المغصوب، ولا يقضى عليه، وقال أبو يوسف يقضى بالدين على المسلم دون الغصب.
Chapter of the Musta'min: If a harbi [and a Muslim] had a debt transaction, or one usurped the other's property and they came out to us [to Dar al-Islam], nothing is adjudged. Similarly, if both were harbis and did that, then sought aman [became musta'min], [no judgment]. But if they came out as Muslims, the debt is adjudged between them, not the usurpation. Because judgment depends on jurisdiction, and we had no jurisdiction over the musta'min at the time of the debt, nor at the time of judgment, since he had not undertaken the rule of Islam regarding his past actions, only for the future. As for usurpation, nothing is adjudged for either party because the property was taken from non-inviolable wealth. However, the Muslim is advised [dinan] to return the usurped property, though not compelled by judgment. Abu Yusuf said: The debt is adjudged against the Muslim, but not the usurpation.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage from the chapter on the musta'min (one who enters Dar al-Islam with safe-conduct) explains the limits of judicial authority over past transactions in Dar al-Harb. The ruling states that if a Muslim and a harbi (or two harbis) conducted a debt transaction or one usurped the other's property while in Dar al-Harb, and then they come to Dar al-Islam as musta'min, the judge does not adjudicate anything regarding those past acts. The reason is that judgment requires jurisdiction (wilaya), and at the time of the transaction, the parties were not subject to Islamic law. The harbi was not a Muslim, and the Muslim's actions abroad are not under the qadi's jurisdiction. If they become Muslims (i.e., the harbi converts) before coming, then the debt can be adjudged because now they are subject to Islamic law and the transaction was valid by mutual consent. However, usurpation remains unjudged because it involved taking property that was not inviolable (ghayr ma'sum) at the time. The Muslim is still advised (yufata) to return the usurped property as a matter of religious duty (dinan), but the judge cannot compel it because the property was not protected by Islamic law at the time of the act. Abu Yusuf's opinion is that the debt is adjudged against the Muslim party but not the usurpation, for the same reasons.

Key Terms
المستأمن (al-musta'min): A person from Dar al-Harb who enters Dar al-Islam with a safe-conduct (aman), thereby temporarily subjecting himself to Islamic law for future acts.
الغصب (al-ghasb): Unlawful taking of another's property by force or stealth, without right.
الولاية (al-wilaya): Judicial authority or jurisdiction to enforce Islamic rulings in courts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling, if a Muslim and a harbi had a debt transaction in Dar al-Harb and then both enter Dar al-Islam as musta'min, what does the judge do?
A) He adjudges the debt because both are now under Islamic law.
B) He does not adjudicate anything because there was no jurisdiction at the time of transaction.
C) He only adjudges the debt if the harbi converts to Islam.
D) He adjudges the usurpation but not the debt.

Key Takeaway
A qadi cannot enforce past transactions from Dar al-Harb because jurisdiction (wilaya) at the time of the act is a necessary condition for judgment.
