Conditions for a Musta'min to Become a Dhimmi
Ruling on musta'min's conversion to dhimmi after one year

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a musta'min becomes a dhimmi in Hanafi fiqh and explain why the imam's statement is necessary.

Core Idea
A musta'min does not become a dhimmi by mere passage of time; the imam must explicitly impose jizya.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq lays out the Hanafi rule that a musta'min (a non-Muslim entering Dar al-Islam with temporary safe conduct) cannot stay permanently unless he accepts dhimmi status. The key condition is that the imam (or his deputy) must warn him that jizya will be imposed if he remains a year. Without this warning, even years of residence do not transform his status. The ruling balances allowing trade and preventing harm to Muslims.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
What is being ruled upon? The passage rules on the status of a musta'min who stays in Dar al-Islam. The ruling: he becomes a dhimmi after one year if the imam imposes jizya.
The act is the musta'min's prolonged residence, and the ruling is his transformation into dhimmi status.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The text uses istihsan (juristic preference) as the proof. It argues that permanent residence harms Muslims (espionage, support for enemies), but preventing all residence blocks trade and food supply. So a middle ground is set: one year and jizya.
No explicit Quran or hadith is quoted; the reasoning is based on public interest (maslaha).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The specific indicators: the phrase 'وقيل له: إن أقمت سنة وضع عليك الجزية' shows the imam's statement is essential. The commentary 'ظاهر في توقف كونه ذمياً على قول الإمام' confirms dependence on the imam's words.
The Year is counted from the imam's statement, not from entry.
4
Note the ikhtilaf (disagreement)
The Shafi'i school holds that jizya is due automatically after one year of residence, without needing the imam's statement. The Hanafi school disagrees, emphasizing the necessity of explicit notification.
This difference affects whether the musta'min can be punished for not paying jizya before being warned.
5
Evaluate the tarjih (preferred view)
The Hanafi position is the mufta bihi, as stated in the text. The analogy with kharaj supports it: merely buying kharaj land does not make one a dhimmi; only undertaking to pay kharaj does.
Thus, action plus explicit commitment is required, not mere time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل
لا يمكن مستأمن فينا سنة، وقيل له: إن أقمت سنة وضع عليك الجزية، فإن مكث بعد سنة فهو ذمي، فلم يترك أن يرجع إليهم كما لو وضع عليه الخراج.
[والأصل فيه أن الكافر لا يمكن من إقامة دائمة في دارنا إلا باسترقاق أو جزية؛ لأنه يبقى ضرراً على المسلمين؛ لكونه جاسوساً لهم، وظهيراً على الأمور علينا ويمكن من الإقامة اليسيرة؛ لأن في منعها قطع المنافع من الطعام والجلب وسد باب التجارات ففصلنا بين المدتين بسنة؛ لأنها مدة تجب فيها الجزية، وقيد بالمستأمن؛ لأنه لو دخل دارنا بلا أمان فهو وما معه فيء لجماعة المسلمين. فينا: أي يقيم في دار الإسلام. وقيل له: أي من قبل الإمام أعم أن يكون القائل هو الإمام أو نائبه إن أقمت سنة: قيد اتفاقي؛ لجواز توقيت ما دونها كشهر وشهرين والجزية بكسر الجيم وسكون المعجمة: اسم للمال يؤخذ من الذمي، من الجزاء بمعنى القضاء؛ لأنه يجزى عن دمه عيني فتح وقيل له: أي للمستأمن من قبل الإمام. فإن مكث بعده: [ أي بعد ما قيل له ذلك. (ط)] هذا ظاهر في توقف كونه ذمياً على قول الإمام أو نائبه حتى أقام سنين من غير أن يقول له شيئاً كان له أن يرجع، ولا جزية في حول المكث إلا بالشرط، وقوله: فهو ذمي أي فيجري القصاص بينه وبين المسلم، ويضمن المسلم قيمة حمره وخنزيره بالإتلاف وتجب الدية عليه بقتله خطأ، ويجب كف الأذى عنه، وتحرم غيبته كالمسلم والذمي من الذمة بمعنى العهد؛ لأنه عاهد المسلمين على ترك الحرب؛ ولأن نقضه يوجب الذم فالمستأمن لما أقام سنة بعد تقدم أمر الإمام إليه صار ملتزماً للجزية، فيصير ذمياً. ثم السنة أقصى هذه المدة وللإمام أن يوقت دون السنة، والأدنى غير مقدرة، بل مفوض إلى رأي الإمام، وقت التقدم إليه لا من وقت دخوله دار الإسلام فلم يترك أن لأن عقد الذمة خلف عن الإسلام فلا ينقض عهدها. (مستخلص فتح عيني إليهم : أي إلى دار الحرب؛ لأن في رجوعه إليهم ضرراً بالمسلمين لعوده حرباً عليهم. (عيني)
وتعتبر المدة من وقت التقدم إليه لا من وقت دخوله دار الإسلام
فلم يترك أن يرجع : كما أي كما لا يترك أن يرجع إليهم. (عيني) كما لو وضع عليه الخراج أي إن دخل الحربي دارنا بأمان، واشتري أرض خراج، ووضع عليه خراج الأرض بأن ألزم به وأخذ عند حلول وقته بمباشرة سببه وهو زراعة الأرض أو التمكن هنا، يصير ذمياً؛ لأن خراج الأرض بمنزلة خراج الرأس، فإذا التزمه صار ملتزماً المقام في دارنا، فأما بمجرد الشراء فلا يصير ذميّا؛ لأن الشراء قد يكون للتجارة، وفيه إشارة إلى أنه لا يصير ذمياً بمجرد المدة وضع الخراج عليه، بل إنما يصير ذميّا بالتزام الخراج وبعد ذلك يلزمه الجزية للسنة المستقبلة، ويعتبر وقت وجوبه، وقيل: يصير ذمياً بنفس الشراء؛ لأنه حينئذ صار ملتزماً حكماً من أحكام الإسلام.
Chapter: A musta'min cannot stay with us for a year, and it is said to him: If you stay a year, jizya is imposed on you. If he remains after the year, he is a dhimmi, so he is not left to return to them, just as if kharaj is imposed on him. [The principle is that a disbeliever cannot be allowed permanent residence in our land except by slavery or jizya; because he remains a harm to Muslims, being a spy for them and an ally against us. He can be allowed limited residence because preventing it cuts off benefits of food and trade. So we distinguish between two periods by a year because that is the duration for which jizya is obligatory. Restricted to musta'min because if he enters our land without aman, he and his property are fay' for the community of Muslims. 'With us': i.e., resides in Dar al-Islam. 'It is said to him': i.e., by the imam or his deputy. 'If you stay a year': a conventional restriction; it is permissible to set a shorter time like a month or two. Jizya: name for wealth taken from dhimmi, from 'jaza' meaning recompense because it compensates for his blood. It is clear that his becoming dhimmi depends on the imam's statement; even if he stays years without being told anything, he can return. There is no jizya in the year of stay except by condition. 'He is a dhimmi': so retaliation applies between him and a Muslim, and a Muslim must pay the value of his donkey and pig if destroyed, and blood money for killing him by mistake, and harm must be withheld from him, and backbiting him is forbidden like a Muslim. Dhimmi from 'dhimma' meaning covenant because he covenanted with Muslims to cease war. The year is the maximum period, and the imam may set less; the minimum is not fixed but left to his opinion. The period is counted from the imam's statement, not from entry. 'So he is not left to return': just as he is not left to return to them. 'Just as if kharaj is imposed': if a harbi enters with aman, buys kharaj land, and kharaj is imposed on him, he becomes a dhimmi because kharaj on land is like kharaj on head; by accepting it he commits to residence. Merely buying does not make him dhimmi because it might be for trade. It indicates he becomes dhimmi only by undertaking kharaj; then jizya is obligatory for the next year. Some say he becomes dhimmi by the purchase itself because he undertakes a ruling of Islam.]
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from principles (istihsan). It states that a musta'min (a harbi who enters Dar al-Islam with aman) cannot be allowed to stay permanently unless he either becomes a slave (through capture) or pays jizya. The reason is that permanent presence could harm Muslims by allowing spying or support for enemies. However, limited residence is permitted to facilitate trade and food supply. The dividing line is one year because jizya becomes due after a year of residence. The imam must explicitly tell the musta'min that if he stays a year, jizya will be imposed. Without this statement, he does not become a dhimmi even if he stays years; he retains the right to return. The commentary emphasizes that the year is the maximum; the imam may set a shorter period. The analogy with kharaj is drawn: just as buying kharaj land leads to becoming dhimmi only after undertaking to pay kharaj (not by mere purchase), similarly staying a year only leads to dhimmi status after the imam's statement and the commitment to jizya. The passage also clarifies the consequences of becoming a dhimmi: full rights and obligations like a Muslim, including retaliation, compensation, and prohibition of backbiting.

Key Terms
مستأمن (musta'min): A harbi (enemy combatant) who enters Dar al-Islam with temporary safe conduct (aman).
ذمي (dhimmi): A non-Muslim subject of the Islamic state who pays jizya and enjoys protection and rights.
جزية (jizya): A poll tax levied on dhimmi men in exchange for protection and exemption from military service.
خراج (kharaj): A land tax imposed on agricultural land in the Islamic state, often on conquered lands remaining in non-Muslim hands.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is required for a musta'min (harbi with aman) to become a dhimmi after one year?
A) Automatic after one year of residence
B) He must marry a Muslim
C) The imam or his deputy must tell him that jizya will be imposed if he stays a year
D) He must buy land

Key Takeaway
A musta'min becomes a dhimmi only after the imam's explicit warning and completion of one year; without the warning, he may return to dar al-harb even after many years.

Inheritance of Apostate's Property: Three Views of Abu Hanifa
Explains distribution of apostate's acquisitions from Islam and apostasy periods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three views of Abu Hanifa on the payment of an apostate's debts from his acquisitions and explain the Sahibayn's opposing position, and identify the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
An apostate's property is split: Islamic acquisitions inherited, apostasy acquisitions become fay'; but which pays which debt? Three Hanafi views.

This passage presents a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute on how to handle the debts and inheritance of an apostate who dies or is killed. Imam Abu Hanifa has three recorded views on whether Islamic debts are paid from Islamic acquisitions or vice versa, while his two disciples hold that both acquisitions are fully owned and all debts are paid jointly.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two types of acquisitions
Identify Kashb al-Islam (acquisitions during Islam) and Kashb al-Ridda (acquisitions during apostasy). Understand that each has a different legal fate.
Kashb al-Islam is inherited by Muslim heirs; Kashb al-Ridda becomes fay' after debt payment.
2
Identify the default ruling
If the apostate dies or is killed, his Islamic acquisitions go to Muslim heirs after paying Islamic debts, and apostasy acquisitions become fay' after paying apostasy debts.
This is the baseline before considering the different views.
3
Examine Abu Hanifa's three views
List the three views: (1) each debt from corresponding acquisition (zahir al-riwayah); (2) Islamic acquisitions first, then apostasy; (3) apostasy acquisitions first, then Islamic. Note the underlying principles.
View 1 is based on 'al-ghurm bi al-ghunm' (profit corresponds to risk).
4
Contrast with Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that both acquisitions are owned by the apostate, so all debts are paid from both jointly, and both are inherited.
This differs from Abu Hanifa's view that apostasy acquisitions are not fully owned.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The first view (each debt from corresponding acquisition) is the apparent transmission (zahir al-riwayah) and is the operative position for fatwa.
The text explicitly states that this is the relied-upon view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن مات، أو قتل على ردّته، ورث كسب إسلامه وارثه المسلم بعد قضاء دين إسلامه. وهو مرتد أو حكم بلحاقه أي وذلك بعد إلخ إلا أنه مدعو إلى الإسلام بالإجبار عليه، ويرجى عوده إليه، فتوقفنا في أمره، فإن أسلم جعل العارض كأن لم يكن في حق هذا الحكم، وصار كأن لم يزل فلم يعمل السبب، وإن مات أو قتل على ردته، أو لحق بدار الحرب وحكم بلحاقه، استقر كفره، فيعمل السبب عمله، وهو إزالة الملك. (مستخلص، فتح) وإن مات إلخ: أي وإن لم يسلم المرتد ومات أو قتل على ردته انتقل ما اكتسبه في الإسلام إلى ورثته المسلمين، وكان ما اكتسبه في حال الردة فيئا وتقضى الديون التي لزمته في الإسلام بما اكتسبه في حالة الإسلام، والديون التي لزمته في الردة بما اكتسبه في حالة الردة وعن أبي حنيفة أنه يبتدأ بكسب الإسلام، فتقضى عنه، فإن لم يف بذلك، تقضى من كسب ردته، وعنه على عكس ذلك، فصارت عنده ثلاثة وجوه: وجه الأول وهو ظاهر الرواية أن المستحق بالسببين مختلف وحصول كل واحد من السببين باعتبار السبب الذي وجب به الدين، فيقضى كل دين من الكسب المكتسب في تلك الحالة، لتكون الغرم بالغنم. ووجه الثاني : أن كسب الإسلام ملكه حتى يخلفه الوارث فيه، ومن شرط هذه الخلافة الفراغ من حق المورث، فيقدم الدين عليه، وكسب الردة ليس بملك له ؛ لبطلان الملك ؛ للردة فلا يقضى دينه إلا إذا تعذر قضاءه من محل آخر، فحينئذ يقضى منه وجه القول الثالث: أن كسب الإسلام حق الورثة وكسب الردة خالص حقه، فكان قضاء دينه منه أولى إلا إذا تعذر بأن لم يف به، فحينئذ يقضى من كسب الإسلام تقديماً لحقه، وأما عندهما، فيقضى ديونه من الكسبين؛ لأنهما جميعاً ملكه وكلا الكسبين موروث لورثته؛ لأن ملكه في الكسبين باق، ولا يزول ملكه كما تقدم، فإذا كان ملكه فينتقل بموته إلى ورثته
If he dies or is killed in his apostasy, his Muslim heir inherits his acquisitions from Islam after paying his Islamic debts. If he is an apostate or if a ruling is made of his joining (dar al-harb), meaning after that etc., except that he is called to Islam by compulsion, and it is hoped he will return to it, so we suspend (judgment) regarding his matter. If he embraces Islam, the obstacle is made as if it did not exist in respect of this ruling, and it becomes as if it never ceased, so the cause does not operate. If he dies or is killed in his apostasy, or joins dar al-harb and a ruling is made of his joining, his disbelief becomes established, so the cause operates: removal of ownership. And if he dies etc.: i.e., if the apostate does not embrace Islam and dies or is killed in his apostasy, what he acquired in Islam transfers to his Muslim heirs, and what he acquired in apostasy is fay', and the debts incurred in Islam are paid from what he acquired in the state of Islam, and the debts incurred in apostasy are paid from what he acquired in the state of apostasy. According to Abu Hanifa, he begins with the Islamic acquisitions and pays from them; if they are insufficient, he pays from his apostasy acquisitions. According to him (Abu Hanifa) also the opposite, so there are three aspects for him: the first aspect, which is the apparent transmission, is that the entitlement from two causes is different, and each cause occurs according to the cause by which the debt was obligated, so each debt is paid from the acquisition acquired in that state, so that profit corresponds to risk. The second aspect: that the Islamic acquisition is his property until the heir inherits it, and a condition of this inheritance is clearance from the right of the decedent, so the debt is given priority over it; and the apostasy acquisition is not his property because ownership is voided by apostasy, so his debt is only paid if it cannot be paid from another source. The third aspect: that the Islamic acquisition is the right of the heirs and the apostasy acquisition is purely his right, so paying his debt from it is more appropriate unless it is insufficient, then it is paid from Islamic acquisitions as a priority for his right. As for according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), his debts are paid from both acquisitions because both are his property and both acquisitions are inherited by his heirs, because his ownership of both acquisitions remains, and his ownership does not cease as previously, so if it is his property, it transfers upon his death to his heirs.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute on how the debts and inheritance of an apostate are handled from his property acquired during Islam and during apostasy. The initial ruling states that if an apostate dies or is killed, his Islamic acquisitions go to his Muslim heirs after paying his Islamic debts; his apostasy acquisitions become fay' (public treasury spoils) after paying his apostasy debts. Imam Abu Hanifa has three recorded views: the first (apparent transmission) is that each debt is paid from the corresponding acquisition (Islamic debts from Islamic acquisitions, apostasy debts from apostasy acquisitions), based on the principle that profit corresponds to risk. The second view prioritizes Islamic acquisitions for Islamic debts, considering apostasy acquisitions as not owned due to apostasy voiding ownership, so apostasy debts are only paid if necessary. The third view prioritizes apostasy acquisitions for apostasy debts because that acquisition is purely his right, while Islamic acquisitions are a right of the heirs. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that both acquisitions are owned and inherited, so all debts are paid jointly from both. This dispute highlights the Hanafi school's nuanced understanding of ownership during apostasy.

Key Terms
كسب الإسلام (kashb al-Islam): Property acquired by the apostate during his state of Islam.
كسب الردة (kashb al-ridda): Property acquired by the apostate during his state of apostasy.
فيء (fay'): Spoils or booty that goes to the public treasury (bayt al-mal) without war.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the apparent transmission of Abu Hanifa, how are the apostate's debts paid from his acquisitions?
A) All debts are paid from Islamic acquisitions first.
B) All debts are paid from apostasy acquisitions first.
C) Each debt is paid from the acquisition acquired in the corresponding state.
D) Debts are not paid; the property becomes fay' immediately.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, apostate's debts are tied to the period they were incurred, with Islamic acquisitions inherited by Muslim heirs and apostasy acquisitions becoming fay'.

Dispute on Apostate's Acquisitions: Shafi'i and Hanafi Positions
Presents Shafi'i view that all apostate's property is fay', with Hanafi response

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on the apostate's property and distinguish it from the Shafi'i position by analyzing the attribution principle.

Core Idea
An apostate's Islamic acquisitions are inherited; apostasy acquisitions become fay' — the key is attribution to the prior Muslim state.

This passage presents a classical inter-madhhab dispute between Imam al-Shafi'i and the Hanafi school regarding the fate of an apostate's property. While Shafi'i rules that all property becomes fay' (public spoils) because the apostate dies a disbeliever, the Hanafi school draws a distinction based on when the property was acquired. Understanding this distinction requires careful consideration of the principle of attribution (istinad) and the temporal dimension of legal status.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the two categories of property: Islamic acquisitions (acquired before apostasy) and apostasy acquisitions (acquired after apostasy). The Hanafi position is that Islamic acquisitions are inherited by Muslim heirs, while apostasy acquisitions become fay'.
This distinction is attributed to Abu Hanifa, building on the attribution principle stated by the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad).
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that all property of the apostate becomes fay' because the apostate dies a disbeliever, and a Muslim cannot inherit from a disbeliever.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The Shafi'i proof is based on the general rule that a Muslim does not inherit a disbeliever, combined with the apostate's status at death as a disbeliever. This leads to the conclusion that all property is fay'.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response introduces the concept of istinad (attribution): the Islamic acquisitions can be attributed to the pre-apostasy period when the person was Muslim, thus allowing inheritance of a Muslim from a Muslim. Apostasy acquisitions cannot be so attributed because they did not exist before apostasy.
The text mentions this argument first for the two companions, then Abu Hanifa adds the explicit distinction between acquisition types.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: In the Hanafi school, Muslim heirs inherit the Islamic acquisitions, while apostasy acquisitions go to the public treasury (bayt al-mal). In the Shafi'i school, all property goes to the public treasury.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الشافعي: كلاهما فيء؛ لأنه مات كافراً، والمسلم لا يرث الكافر. حربي ثم بقي مال لا أمان لهم، فيكون فيئاً، ولهما أن الإرث فيه يستند إلى ما قبل ردته؛ إذ الردة سبب الموت، فيكون توريث المسلم من المسلم، ولأبي حنيفة: أنه يمكن الاستناد في كسب الإسلام؛ لوجوده قبل الردة، ولا يمكن الاستناد في كسب الردة؛ لعدمه قبلها
And al-Shafi'i said: Both are fay' because he died as a disbeliever, and a Muslim does not inherit a disbeliever. Harbi, then property remains with no security for them, so it becomes fay'. And for them (the two companions) that inheritance in it is attributed to the period before his apostasy, because apostasy is the cause of death, so it is inheritance of a Muslim from a Muslim. And for Abu Hanifa: that attribution is possible in the acquisition from Islam because it existed before apostasy, but attribution is not possible in the acquisition from apostasy because it did not exist before it.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the apostate's property. Imam al-Shafi'i argues that both types of acquisitions (Islamic and apostasy) are fay' (public spoils) because the apostate dies a disbeliever, and a Muslim cannot inherit from a disbeliever. The Hanafi response, attributed to the companions and Abu Hanifa, distinguishes: the Islamic acquisition can be attributed to the pre-apostasy period when the person was Muslim, so inheritance from a Muslim to a Muslim is valid; the apostasy acquisition cannot be so attributed, hence becomes fay'. This shows the Hanafi principle of considering the cause of death (apostasy) while also respecting the prior state of Islam for property acquired during that state.

Key Terms
فيء (fay'): Property that becomes spoils for the public treasury without battle.
استناد (istinad): The legal attribution of an event or property to a particular time or state, used here to connect the apostate's Islamic acquisitions to his pre-apostasy Muslim status.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does Abu Hanifa allow inheritance of the apostate's Islamic acquisitions?
A) Because the apostate repented before death.
B) Because the Islamic acquisitions can be attributed to the time before apostasy when he was Muslim.
C) Because Islamic acquisitions are less valuable.
D) Because the apostate is still considered a Muslim for property matters.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes apostate property by time of acquisition: Islamic acquisitions are inherited, apostasy acquisitions become fay'.

Husband's Inheritance from Female Apostate: Conditions and Reasoning
States conditional inheritance for husband of apostate wife

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for a husband to inherit from his apostate wife and explain the ta'lil (legal reasoning) based on the concept of farr.

Core Idea
A husband inherits from an apostate wife only if she was ill at apostasy and died during idda — a healthy female apostate is not executed and thus not a 'fleer' from inheritance.

This passage presents a nuanced ruling on inheritance from a female apostate. While her property always goes to her heirs by consensus, the husband's inheritance is conditional. The key distinction hinges on whether the apostasy was a deliberate act to deprive heirs (farr), which applies to male apostates due to execution, but to females only if they were terminally ill at the time.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is inheritance by the husband from his apostate wife. The ruling: he inherits only if she apostatized while ill and died during her idda (waiting period). Otherwise, he does not inherit.
The idda for a woman after divorce or death of husband is typically three menstrual cycles or three months; for apostasy, it is the same period.
2
List the conditions for the husband to inherit
From the text: (1) She must be a female apostate. (2) She must have been ill (i.e., terminally ill) at the moment of apostasy. (3) She must die during the idda period after apostasy. If any condition is missing, the husband does not inherit.
3
Understand the reasoning (ta'lil)
The text explains why a healthy female apostate does not cause inheritance: because she is not killed, so she is not considered a 'farr' (one who flees from Islam to deprive heirs). In contrast, a male apostate is killed if he does not repent, so even if healthy, he is considered a farr. This reasoning is analogized to divorce during terminal illness, where the husband's intent to deprive the wife of inheritance is presumed.
The concept of farr is key: it presumes intent to deprive rightful heirs.
4
Compare with male apostate
The passage contrasts the female with the male apostate: the male is executed (unless he repents), so he becomes a farr regardless of his health at apostasy. Thus, his property may be treated differently — but the passage focuses only on the female.
5
Note the consensus on property ownership
The text states that the female apostate's property, whether acquired in Islam or during apostasy, goes to her heirs. This is agreed upon by the mentioned schools (Malik, Ahmad, al-Shafi'i).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال مالك وأحمد مثل قول الشافعي وكسب المرتدة سواء كان كسب الإسلام أو الردة ،لورثتها، وهذا بالاتفاق ويرثها زوجها إن ارتدت وهي مريضة وماتت في العدة، وإن كانت صحيحة لا يرثها؛ لأنها لا تقتل فلا تصير فارة بالردة بخلاف المرتد، فإنه يصير فاراً بالردة وإن كان صحيحاً؛ لوجوب القتل، فأشبه الطلاق في مرض الموت.
And Malik and Ahmad said similar to the statement of al-Shafi'i, and the acquisition of the female apostate, whether it is acquisition from Islam or apostasy, goes to her heirs, and this is by agreement. And her husband inherits from her if she apostatized while ill and died during the idda (waiting period), but if she was healthy, he does not inherit from her, because she is not killed, so she does not become a fleer (farr) by apostasy, unlike the male apostate, for he becomes a fleer by apostasy even if healthy, because killing is obligatory, so it resembles divorce during terminal illness.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage provides a ruling specific to the female apostate (murtadda). By consensus, her property, whether acquired in Islam or apostasy, goes to her heirs. The husband's inheritance is conditional: he inherits only if she apostatized while terminally ill and subsequently died during her idda (waiting period after divorce or death). If she was healthy at the time of apostasy, the husband does not inherit. The reasoning is that a healthy female apostate is not executed (unlike a male apostate who is killed unless he repents), so she is not considered to have fled from Islam to deprive her heirs. This analogy is drawn from the rule of divorce during terminal illness, where a divorcing husband's intent to deprive the wife of inheritance is presumed.

Key Terms
فارة (fārrah): One who flees; here, the apostate is considered to have fled from Islam to deprive heirs of inheritance.
مرتدة (murtadda): Female apostate.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, under what condition does a husband inherit from his apostate wife?
A) If she apostatized while healthy and repented.
B) If she apostatized while ill and died during idda.
C) If she apostatized and was imprisoned.
D) If she apostatized and her husband also apostatized.

Key Takeaway
A husband inherits from his apostate wife only if she was terminally ill at apostasy and died during her idda; otherwise, she is not considered a 'farr' (fleeing from inheritance).

Three Hanafi Narrations on Who Inherits the Apostate
Presents three narrations from Abu Hanifa on determining the heir of the apostate

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three narrated positions from Abu Hanifa regarding who inherits the apostate, identify the most correct position, and explain the practical difference.

Core Idea
The heir of the apostate is determined at death, not at apostasy – this is the most correct Hanafi position.

The Hanafi school preserves multiple narrations (riwayat) from Imam Abu Hanifa on many issues. This passage presents three such narrations on a specific question: at what point in time should the heir of an apostate be determined? Understanding these narrations and knowing which is relied upon for fatwa is essential for correctly applying inheritance rules in cases of apostasy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three narrations
Read the Arabic passage carefully. List each narrator (al-Hasan, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) and their reported view on when the heir is determined.
Each narration is a riwayah from Abu Hanifa through a specific student.
2
Compare the timing of determination
For each narration, note the exact moment at which the heir's status is fixed: at apostasy, at apostasy but not invalidated later, or at death.
Abu Yusuf's narration is close to al-Hasan's but differs on whether the heir's status can be invalidated.
3
Identify the most correct position
The text explicitly states that Muhammad's narration is 'al-ashah' (the most correct). Understand that this is the mufta bihi position.
Tarjih is based on strength of evidence, though the text does not elaborate on the reasoning here.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: if the heir dies after apostasy but before the apostate, under al-Hasan's view the heir's heirs would inherit; under Muhammad's view, a new heir at death would inherit.
This affects actual distribution of the apostate's estate.
5
Understand intra-madhhab ikhtilaf
All three narrations are from Abu Hanifa, so this is a dispute within the Hanafi school itself, not between schools.
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) differ from each other and from al-Hasan.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اختلفت الروايات عن أبي حنيفة فيمن يرث المرتد، فروى الحسن عنه أنه يرثه كان وارثاً له وقت ردته، من كذلك إلى وقت موته أو قتله أو القضاء بلحاقه، وروى أبو يوسف أنه يعتبر وجوده وقت الردة، ولا يبطل بموته أو بشيء آخر قبل موت المرتد، وروى محمد عنه أنه يعتبر كونه وارثاً عند موت المرتد أو قتله، أو القضاء بلحاقه سواء كان موجوداً عند الردة، أو حدث بعده وهو الأصح. (مستخلص، فتح)
Then the narrations from Abu Hanifa differed regarding who inherits the apostate. Al-Hasan narrated from him that the one who is an heir at the time of his apostasy inherits from him, continuing until his death or being killed or the ruling of his joining (dar al-harb). Abu Yusuf narrated that existence (as an heir) is considered at the time of apostasy, and it is not invalidated by his death or anything else before the death of the apostate. Muhammad narrated from him that being an heir is considered at the death of the apostate or his being killed or the ruling of his joining, whether he existed at the time of apostasy or occurred after, and this is the most correct.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage discusses three narrations (riwayat) from Imam Abu Hanifa regarding who is eligible to inherit from an apostate. According to the narration of al-Hasan ibn Ziyad, the heir is determined at the time of apostasy, and that person continues to be the heir until the apostate's death. According to Abu Yusuf, the existence of the heir at the time of apostasy is considered, and this heir does not lose entitlement even if other heirs appear later. According to Muhammad al-Shaybani, the heir is determined at the time of death (or being killed or ruling of lahaq), regardless of whether they were an heir at the time of apostasy. The text states that the position of Muhammad is the most correct (al-ashah).

Key Terms
رواية (riwayah): A narration or transmission of a legal opinion from Abu Hanifa through a specific student.
الأصح (al-ashah): The most correct or soundest position among multiple narrations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the most correct position (al-ashah), when is the heir of the apostate determined?
A) At the time of apostasy.
B) At the time of death or joining dar al-harb.
C) At the time of apostasy and maintained regardless of death.
D) Only if the heir existed before apostasy.

Key Takeaway
The most correct Hanafi position (al-ashah) is that the heir of the apostate is determined at the time of his death, not at apostasy.

Rulings When Apostate Joins Dar al-Harb and Transaction Categories
Explains effects of court ruling of lahaq and categories of apostate's transactions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the legal effects of a judge's ruling of lahaq (apostate joining dar al-harb) according to the Hanafi school and distinguish the four categories of apostate transactions.

Core Idea
Lahaq makes apostate like a harbi dead; a judge's ruling triggers slave liberation, debt acceleration, and transaction suspension.

The apostate who flees to enemy territory severs ties with Islamic jurisdiction, but how does the law treat his pending obligations and transactions? This lesson unpacks the Hanafi ruling on lahaq—the court's declaration that an apostate has joined dar al-harb—and the ensuing legal effects on his property, family, and transactions. We will also explore the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf and the Shafi'i counter-position, as well as the comprehensive categorization of all apostate transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three main effects of a lahaq ruling
From the passage, extract the three consequences when a judge rules that an apostate has joined dar al-harb: (1) freeing of mudabbar and umm walad, (2) immediate due of deferred debts, (3) suspension of certain transactions (sale, gift, emancipation). Note the difference: mudabbar from one third; umm walad from all property.
The freeing of umm walad is from the entire estate, reflecting her higher status.
2
Understand the underlying analogy (qiyas) to a harbi's death
The Hanafi proof: by joining dar al-harb, the apostate becomes like a harbi, who is treated as 'dead' with respect to Islamic rulings because the authority of Islamic law over him is severed, just as it is for a deceased person. This qiyas jaliy connects lahaq to death, but with the condition of a judge's ruling due to the possibility of return.
The Shafi'i objection maintains that the world is one dar, so no death-like consequences arise without actual death.
3
Trace the intra-Hanafi dispute on transaction suspension
Abu Hanifa holds that sale, purchase, and similar transactions are suspended (mawquf) – effective if the apostate returns to Islam, void if he perishes. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) argue these transactions are valid immediately, though with nuances: Abu Yusuf treats them as from a healthy person, Muhammad as from a sick person.
The difference stems from the principle of whether apostasy removes ownership in a contingent (mura'a) or absolute manner.
4
Classify all apostate transactions into the four categories
The passage provides a complete taxonomy: (1) Effective by consensus: istilad, divorce, accepting gifts, etc.; (2) Invalid by consensus: marriage, slaughter, inheritance; (3) Suspended by consensus: partnership, dealing with minor's property; (4) Disputed: sale, purchase – the disagreement is between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.
This classification helps determine the validity of any action by an apostate in different contexts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكسب ردّته فيء بعد قضاء دين ردّته. وإن حُكم بلحاقه عتق مدبّره وأم ولده، أي حكم الحاكم من الثلث من كل ماله وحلّ دينه، وتوقف مبايعته وعتقه ،وهبته، فإن آمن نفذ، وإن هلك بطل، قضاء أي يكون موقوفا أي المرتد تفسير لقوله توقف ما كان موقوفا وكسب ردته أي الذي اكتسبه في حال الردة (عيني) قيء: أي غنيمة توضع في بيت المال. (معدن) دين: أي الدين الذي ركبه في حال ردته. (عيني) وإن حكم بلحاقه أي إذا لحق المرتد بدار الحرب مرتداً، وحكم الحاكم بلحاق المرتد بدار الحرب، عتق مدبره من الثلث وكذا مدبرها إذا لحقت، وعتق أم ولده من كل ماله وحل دينه أي الذي كان عليه على سبيل التأجيل، فيؤخذ منه في الحال، ويبطل التأجيل، ونقل ما اكتسبه في حالة الإسلام إلى ورثته المسلمين، وقال الشافعي: يبقى ماله موقوفاً بناء على أصله أن الدنيا كلها دار واحدة عنده، فلا يعتق مدبره ولا أم ولده ولا يحل دينه الذي عليه؛ لأنه حقيقة و به قال مالك وأحمد، ولنا: أنه باللحاق صار من أهل الحرب، وهم أموات في حق أحكام أهل الإسلام لانقطاع ولاية الإلزام كما انقطعت الموتى، فصار كالموت إلا أنه لا يتقرر لحاقه إلا بحكم الحاكم؛ لاحتمال أن يعود إلينا، فلابد من وهو ظاهر الرواية، وفي بعض الروايات تثبت الأحكام بمجرد الالتحاق. عن حي القضاء، وقوله: "عتق مدبره إشارة إلى أن أحكام الموت تتحقق بمجرد الحكم باللحاق ولا يشترط القضاء بتلك الأحكام، وبه قال الجمهور، وإليه أشار محمد في أكثر المواضع، وقيل: يشترط القضاء بشيء من أحكام الموتى، ولا يكتفى بالقضاء باللحاق. (مستخلص، فتح) دينه أي الدين المؤجل الذي عليه؛ لأن اللحاق كالموت، وأما دينه الموجل الذي له فيبقى على أجله. (ط، عيني) توقف مبايعته أراد بالمبايعة كل ما كان مبادلة مال بمال فشمل الصرف والسلم والصلح عن إقرار والإجارة وقبض الدين؛ لأنه مبادلة حكمية، والرهن؛ لأنه معاوضة مالية والهبة بشرط العوض. وقوله: "وعتقه" أي مع توابعه كالتدبير والكتابة وقوله: فإن آمن نفذ وإن هلك بطل"، هذا عند أبي حنيفة وقالا يجوز هذه التصرفات، عاد إلى الإسلام أو لم يعد، وهذا على الأصل المذكور، وهو أن الردة تزيل الملك زوالاً مراعي عنده خلافاً لهما. ثم اعلم أن تصرفات المرتد على أربعة أنواع: الأول: نافذ بالاتفاق كالاستيلاد والطلاق وقبول الهبة وتسليم الشفعة والحجر على عبده المأذون؛ لأنها لا تستدعي الولاية. الثاني: باطل بالاتفاق كالنكاح والذبيحة والإرث؛ لأنها تعتمد الملة ولا ملة له. الثالث موقوف بالاتفاق كالمفاوضة والتصرف على ولده الصغير، ومال ولده؛ لأنها تعتمد المساواة، ولا مساواة بين المسلم والمرتد مالم يسلم والرابع مختلف فيه كالبيع والشراء تتوقف هذه التصرفات عنده وعندهما تنفذ إلا عند أبي يوسف يصح كما يصح من الصحيح، وعند محمد: ينفذ كما ينفذ من المريض. (مستخلص، فتح عيني) وهبته: ورهنه وتصرفه في ماله. هلك أي على ردته بأن مات أو قتل أو لحق بدار الحرب. (عيني)
And his apostasy acquisition becomes fay' after paying his apostasy debt. And if a ruling is made of his joining (dar al-harb), his mudabbar (slave promised freedom upon owner's death) and umm walad (concubine mother of his child) are freed from one third from all his property, and his debt becomes due, and his sale, emancipation, and gift are suspended; if he becomes secure (returns to Islam), they are effective; if he perishes (on apostasy), they are void. Ruling: i.e., the apostate is suspended. Explanation of his statement 'suspended what was suspended'. And his apostasy acquisition: i.e., what he acquired in the state of apostasy (literally: 'ayni') fay': i.e., spoils placed in the public treasury. Debt: i.e., the debt he incurred in his apostasy. And if a ruling of his joining: i.e., if the apostate joins dar al-harb as an apostate, and the judge rules his joining dar al-harb, his mudabbar is freed from one third, and similarly his mudabbara if she joined, and his umm walad is freed from all his property, and his debt becomes due, i.e., the deferred debt he owed is taken immediately, and deferral is void, and what he acquired in the state of Islam is transferred to his Muslim heirs. And al-Shafi'i said: His property remains suspended based on his principle that the whole world is one dar for him, so his mudabbar and umm walad are not freed, nor his debt becomes due, because he is literally (alive). And Malik and Ahmad said likewise. Our evidence: that by joining he has become one of the people of war, and they are like dead regarding the rulings of the people of Islam because the authority of compulsion is severed as it is severed for the dead, so it becomes like death except that his joining is only established by a judge's ruling because of the possibility of his return to us, so it is necessary (for the ruling), and this is the apparent transmission, and in some transmissions the rulings are established merely by joining. Regarding the living and the judge, and his statement: 'his mudabbar is freed' indicates that the rulings of death are actualized merely by the ruling of joining, and it is not necessary to rule on those specific rulings, and the majority hold this, and Muhammad alluded to this in most places. And it is said: it is necessary to rule on some death rulings, and not satisfied with the ruling of joining. His debt: i.e., the deferred debt he owes, because joining is like death; as for his deferred debt that is owed to him, it remains on its term. Suspension of his sale: by 'sale' is meant everything that is exchange of property for property, so it includes currency exchange, salam, settlement on acknowledgement, lease, and recovery of debt because it is a virtual exchange, and pledge because it is a financial exchange, and gift with compensation. And his statement: 'and his emancipation' i.e., with its subsidiaries like tadbir and kitaba. And his statement: 'if he becomes secure, they are effective; if he perishes, they are void' – this is according to Abu Hanifa, while the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say these transactions are valid whether he returns to Islam or not, and this is based on the mentioned principle that apostasy removes ownership in a contingent manner according to him (Abu Hanifa), contrary to them. Then know that the apostate's transactions are of four types: First: effective by agreement, like istilad (making a slave mother of a child), divorce, accepting a gift, surrendering pre-emption, and placing interdiction on his authorized slave, because these do not require guardianship. Second: invalid by agreement, like marriage, slaughter, and inheritance, because they depend on religion and he has no religion. Third: suspended by agreement, like partnership (mufawada) and dealing with his minor child and his child's property, because they depend on equality, and there is no equality between Muslim and apostate unless he embraces Islam. Fourth: disputed, like sale and purchase; these are suspended according to him (Abu Hanifa), while according to the two companions they are effective, except that according to Abu Yusuf they are valid as from a sound person, and according to Muhammad they are effective as from a sick person. His gifts: and his pledge and his disposal of his property. He perishes: i.e., in his apostasy, whether by death, being killed, or joining dar al-harb.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage covers the effects when a court rules that an apostate has joined dar al-harb (lahaq). Three key effects: (1) his mudabbar and umm walad are freed – the mudabbar from one third of the estate, the umm walad from all property; (2) his deferred debts become immediately due; (3) his transactions (sale, gift, emancipation) are suspended, effective if he returns to Islam, void if he dies or is killed on apostasy. The Hanafi evidence is that joining dar al-harb makes him like a harbi, who is considered 'dead' regarding Islamic rulings because the authority to enforce laws is severed, analogous to death. However, this status requires a judge's ruling due to the possibility of return. The passage then categorizes all of an apostate's transactions into four types: (1) effective by consensus (e.g., istilad, divorce); (2) invalid by consensus (e.g., marriage, slaughter); (3) suspended by consensus (e.g., partnership); (4) disputed (sale and purchase): Abu Hanifa says suspended; the Sahibayn say effective, with Abu Yusuf treating it like from a healthy person and Muhammad like from a sick person. This shows the Hanafi school's detailed classification of the apostate's legal capacity.

Key Terms
لحاق (lahaq): Joining dar al-harb (enemy territory) as an apostate, subject to a judge's ruling.
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave promised freedom upon the master's death (tadbir).
أم ولد (umm walad): A slave concubine who has borne her master a child, free upon his death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) They are immediately void.
B) They are suspended; effective if he returns to Islam, void if he perishes.
C) They are valid regardless of his return or death.
D) They are valid only if the buyer is Muslim.

Key Takeaway
Lahaq (apostate joining dar al-harb) triggers a legal status analogous to death, requiring a judge's ruling, with specific effects on slaves, debts, and transactions, and an intra-Hanafi dispute on transaction validity.

Ownership of Apostate's Wealth in Dar al-Harb
Ruling with proof on apostate's property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on an apostate's wealth that he takes to dar al-harb, and distinguish the two narrations regarding the timing of heirs' ownership.

Core Idea
An apostate's wealth taken to dar al-harb becomes spoils; wealth left behind goes to heirs from the moment of joining.

This passage addresses a nuanced issue: what happens to the property of an apostate who flees to enemy territory? The Hanafi school distinguishes between wealth he takes with him and wealth he leaves behind. The key turning point is the judge's decree versus the mere act of joining. We will unpack the two narrations and the authoritative position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Scenario A: apostate takes wealth to dar al-harb and Muslims conquer it. Scenario B: apostate leaves wealth behind, returns, then takes it again to dar al-harb.
2
State the ruling for each
Scenario A: wealth becomes spoils (fay'). Scenario B: wealth goes to heirs.
The basis is when heirs become owners.
3
Analyze the ikhtilaf
Two narrations: first says heirs own from time of joining; second says only after judge's decree. The first is zahir al-riwaya.
The judge's decree only strengthens certainty of non-return.
4
Determine mufta bihi
The first narration is relied upon: heirs own from the moment of joining, even without decree.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن لحق المرتدّ بماله، فظهر عليه فهو فيء، فإن رجع إلى دار الإسلام وذهب أي مع ماله بدار الحرب المال أي المال بمال، فظهر عليه فلوارثه، فإن الحق وقضي بعبده لابنه، فكاتبه، فجاء مسلماً، ..... أي على المال خير المرتد وهو ابنه: أي المرتد لصحة الاستيلاد فلا يرثه أي الولد لا يرث المرتد مع ثبوت نسبه منه. فهو فيء: أي إذا لحق المرتد بدار الحرب مع ماله فظهرنا على الدار وعلى مال فماله غنيمة، يعني ليس لورثته سبيل عليه؛ لأن ملكهم ثابت فيه حيث ألحقه به ابتداء، وكذا إن أخرجه تاجر، بخلاف نفسه حيث لا يكون فيئاً؛ لأن المرتد لا يسترق فلم يكن أهلا لأن يكون فيئا. [ رمز الحقائق: ١/٤٤٥] (عيني، مستخلص) فإن رجع : [ أي المرتد بعد الحاقه بدار الحرب بلا مال، سواء قضى بلحاقه أو لا. (ط) أي لحق المرتد بدار الحرب ثم رجع وأخذ مالاً . ولحق بدار الحرب فظهرنا على الدار ، فماله لوارثه؛ لأنه لما لحق أولا بدار الحرب ملكته الورثة، فلهم أن يأخذوه قبل القسمة بغير شيء، وبعدها بالعوض، وكذا لو اشتراه التاجر يأخذونه بالعوض، هذا إذا رجع بعد قضاء القاضي بلحاقه. وأما إذا رجع قبل الحكم به ولحق به ثانيا فكذلك في رواية؛ لأن القضاء إنما شرط ليترجح جانب عدم الرجوع إلى دارنا، ولما خرج إلينا مغيراً بماله إلى دار الحرب ظهر أنه لا يريد العود إلى دارنا فتقرر موته بدون القضاء، ورجع . فيصير ماله لورثته من حين اللحاق وفي رواية يكون فيئا؛ لأنه بمجرد اللحاق لا يصير الوارث مالكا فلا سبيل لورثته على ذلك المال، والرواية الأولى هي . ظاهر الرواية وهو الوجه [ رمز الحقائق ١٢/٤٤٥] (مستخلص، فتح) فلوارثه أي فماله لوارثه إلا أنه يأخذه بغير شيء قبل القسمة وبالقيمة بعدها. (ط، عيني) لحق: أي المرتد بدار الحرب وترك عبداً في دار الإسلام. (عيني) فجاء مسلماً : أي المرتد إلى دار الإسلام حال كونه مسلماً. (عيني) فالمكاتبة والولاء لمورثه أي إذا لحق المرتد بدار الحرب، وترك عبداً في دار الإسلام، فقضى به لابن المرتد، فكاتبه الابن، ثم جاء المرتد مسلماً، فبدل الكتابة والولاء للمرتد الذي أسلم؛ لأن ملك الوارث خلف عن ملك المورث؛ لاستغنائه، فإذا جاء مسلماً تبين أنه محتاج إليه، فيعاد إليه ملكه، ولا وجه إلى بطلان الكتابة، لصدورها عن ولاية شرعية، فجعل الابن كالوكيل من جهته، وحقوق هذا العقد ترجع إلى الموكل فوقع العتق عن المورث الموكل، والولاء لمن أعتق أي لمن يقع عنه العتق ونظيره المكاتب إذا كاتب عبده ثم عجز وفسخت الكتابة الأولى، تبقى الكتابة الثانية على حالها، ويكون بدل الكتابة وولاؤه لمولاه، بخلاف ما إذا رجع بعد أداء الكتابة؛ لأن الملك الذي كان له غير قائم بعد الأداء، وكذا إذا باع الابن العبد فيكون الولاء وثمنه للابن. (مستخلص، عيني) لمورثه: أي لمورث الابن وهو المرتد الذي جاء مسلماً؛ بخلاف ما إذا رجع بعد ما عتق المكاتب فإن الولاء للابن.
If the apostate joins the abode of war with his wealth, and we overcome it, then it is spoils. Then if he returns to the abode of Islam and goes with his wealth to the abode of war, then it is for his heir. Then the judge decreed his slave to his son, so he made a kitabah with him, then he came as a Muslim... the apostate's choice regarding the wealth. And he is his son: meaning the apostate's child due to valid parentage, so he does not inherit from him. So it is spoils: i.e., if the apostate joins dar al-harb with his wealth and we overcome the abode and the wealth, his wealth is booty, meaning his heirs have no claim to it; because their ownership is not established since he attached it to dar al-harb initially. Similarly if a merchant took it out (of dar al-Islam), contrary to his person, which is not spoils because the apostate is not enslaved. (Al-'Ayni, Mustakhlas) Then if he returns: i.e., the apostate after joining dar al-harb without wealth, whether the judge decreed his joining or not. (Tahawi) i.e., he joined dar al-harb then returned and took wealth, and joined dar al-harb again, and we overcame the abode, then his wealth is for his heir; because when he first joined dar al-harb, the heirs owned it (the wealth he left behind). So they may take it before division without anything, and after division with compensation. Similarly if a merchant bought it, they take it with compensation. This is if he returned after the judge's decree of joining. As for if he returned before the decree and joined a second time, it is likewise in one narration; because the decree is only a condition to strengthen the side of non-return to our abode, and since he came out to us (then) changed (his intention?) and took his wealth to dar al-harb, it appears he does not intend to return to our abode, so his death is established without decree and his wealth goes to his heirs from the time of joining. In another narration it is spoils; because by mere joining the heir does not become owner, so his heirs have no recourse to that wealth. The first narration is the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya) and it is the correct view. (Mustakhlas, Fath) So it is for his heir: i.e., his wealth is for his heir, except that he takes it before division without anything, and after division for its value. (Tahawi, 'Ayni) He joined: i.e., the apostate joined dar al-harb and left a slave in dar al-Islam. ('Ayni) So he came as a Muslim: i.e., the apostate came to dar al-Islam while being Muslim. ('Ayni) So the kitabah (contract) and wala' (clientage) are for his heir: i.e., if the apostate joined dar al-harb and left a slave in dar al-Islam, and the judge decreed the slave to the son of the apostate, and the son made a kitabah contract with him, then the apostate came as a Muslim, then the kitabah payment and wala' go to the apostate who became Muslim; because the heir's ownership is a substitute for the testator's ownership due to his (the testator's) independence (i.e., wealth), and when he comes as a Muslim it becomes clear that he is in need of it, so his ownership is returned to him. There is no reason to invalidate the kitabah, because it was issued upon a valid authority, so the son is considered a proxy on his behalf, and the rights of this contract revert to the principal; so the emancipation occurs from the principal and wala' belongs to the one who emancipates. An example is a mukatib who makes a kitabah with his slave then the slave fails and the first kitabah is rescinded; the second kitabah remains as is, and the payment and wala' go to his master. Contrary to the case where he returns after the payment of the kitabah; because the ownership he had is no longer extant after the payment. Similarly, if the son sold the slave, then wala' and the price belong to the son. (Mustakhlas, 'Ayni) For his heir: i.e., the heir of the son, who is the apostate who came as a Muslim; contrary to if he returned after the mukatib became free, then wala' belongs to the son.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage deals with the property of an apostate who joins dar al-harb (the enemy territory) after apostasy. According to the Hanafi school, if an apostate takes his movable wealth into dar al-harb and Muslims later conquer that territory, that wealth becomes ghanimah (spoils) because the apostate's ownership is terminated and his heirs have no claim. However, if the apostate returns to dar al-Islam without his wealth, then goes back to dar al-harb with wealth, and Muslims conquer it, the wealth goes to his heirs because his initial departure without wealth made his heirs owners of the property he left behind. The text discusses the role of the judge's decree in determining death/loss. The second part addresses a scenario where the apostate left a slave in dar al-Islam, the judge gave the slave to the apostate's Muslim son, the son made a kitabah contract with the slave for emancipation, and then the apostate returns as a Muslim. In this case, the kitabah payments and wala' belong to the returning apostate (the original owner) because the son acted as a proxy; the contract is valid and emancipation is attributed to the original owner. The commentary provides an analogy with a mukatib (master) who makes a kitabah with his slave and then rescinds it. The relied-upon position is that the first narration (heirs own the wealth from the time of joining) is the authoritative one.

Key Terms
فيء (fay'): Spoils taken from enemies without fighting, or property of apostates that becomes booty.
لحاق (lahaq): The act of joining dar al-harb; used here as a legal trigger for inheritance and spoils.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi narration, when do heirs become owners of an apostate's wealth left behind in dar al-Islam?
A) Only after a judge decrees the apostate's death
B) From the moment the apostate joins dar al-harb
C) Only after Muslims conquer dar al-harb
D) Never, because it becomes spoils

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school establishes heirs' ownership of an apostate's abandoned wealth from the moment of his joining dar al-harb, and this is the mufta bihi position.

Kitabah Contract of Apostate's Slave Upon Return
Ruling with proof on kitabah contract

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on the kitabah contract made by an apostate's son when the apostate returns as a Muslim, and identify who is entitled to the payments and wala'.

Core Idea
A kitabah contract made by the apostate's son is valid and benefits the returning apostate as principal.

This lesson deals with a more complex scenario: an apostate leaves a slave in dar al-Islam, the judge awards the slave to his son, the son makes a kitabah contract with the slave for emancipation, and then the apostate returns as a Muslim. Who gets the kitabah payments and the wala'? The Hanafi ruling applies the principle of proxy (wakalah).

Guided Path
1
Understand the scenario
Apostate leaves a slave in dar al-Islam. Judge decrees slave to apostate's son. Son makes kitabah with slave. Then apostate returns as Muslim.
2
Identify the legal roles
Apostate: original owner (principal). Son: heir and proxy (wakil). Slave: mukatib.
Son's ownership is a substitute (khalaf) for the apostate's ownership.
3
Apply the proxy principle
Since the son acted on valid authority, the contract is valid. When apostate returns, his ownership revives, so the son becomes a proxy.
The contract rights revert to the principal (apostate).
4
Determine the entitlements
Kitabah payments and wala' belong to the apostate (principal). Exception: if slave becomes free before apostate's return, wala' stays with son.

Source Text

فإن لحق المرتدّ بماله، فظهر عليه فهو فيء، فإن رجع إلى دار الإسلام وذهب أي مع ماله بدار الحرب المال أي المال بمال، فظهر عليه فلوارثه، فإن الحق وقضي بعبده لابنه، فكاتبه، فجاء مسلماً، ..... أي على المال خير المرتد وهو ابنه: أي المرتد لصحة الاستيلاد فلا يرثه أي الولد لا يرث المرتد مع ثبوت نسبه منه. فهو فيء: أي إذا لحق المرتد بدار الحرب مع ماله فظهرنا على الدار وعلى مال فماله غنيمة، يعني ليس لورثته سبيل عليه؛ لأن ملكهم ثابت فيه حيث ألحقه به ابتداء، وكذا إن أخرجه تاجر، بخلاف نفسه حيث لا يكون فيئاً؛ لأن المرتد لا يسترق فلم يكن أهلا لأن يكون فيئا. This passage deals with the property of an apostate who joins dar al-harb (the enemy territory) after apostasy. Key Terms
مكاتبة (mukatabah): A contract of emancipation between a master and slave, where the slave pays installments for freedom.
ولاء (wala'): Clientage; the bond of patronage between the emancipator and the freed slave, which includes inheritance rights and responsibility for crimes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the scenario where an apostate returns as a Muslim after his son made a kitabah contract with the apostate's slave, who receives the kitabah payments?
A) The son, because he made the contract
B) The apostate, because his ownership revives
C) The slave keeps them
D) They are given to charity

Key Takeaway
When an apostate returns as a Muslim, his ownership revives retroactively, and any kitabah contract made by his heir is attributed to him as principal.

Using Rebel Weapons and Horses in Battle
Ruling on Imam using rebel property when needed, with proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state when the Imam may use rebel (bughat) property without consent and explain the Hanafi proof from necessity and precedent.

Core Idea
Necessity in battle justifies temporary use of rebel property.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses a practical battlefield question: may the Imam take weapons and horses from rebels during combat? The author rules that when there is need, temporary use is permissible, citing the actions of the Prophet (SAW) and Ali (RA) as precedents. This lesson unpacks the condition of need, the proof, and the difference between rebel and ordinary Muslim property.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What exactly is being ruled upon? Using rebel weapons and horses during battle. The ruling is that it is permissible (ja'iz) when the Imam needs them.
The act is temporary use, not ownership or spoils.
2
Locate the dalil
What proof does the author cite? The actions of the Prophet (SAW) taking a shield as a loan and Ali (RA) distributing weapons at the Battle of Basra. These are considered sound precedents for permissibility.
The dalil is not a direct Quranic verse but validated through istihsan.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How do these actions prove the ruling? They show that temporary use of rebel property is allowed when needed, as the Prophet and Ali did not seek permission but acted on necessity.
The key is the temporary nature—no ownership is transferred.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
What is the opposing view? Imam al-Shafi'i holds that a Muslim's property cannot be used without consent, even in battle. The Hanafi response is that rebels are not ordinary Muslims; their rebellion weakens property rights in the context of necessity.
The practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) is whether consent is required.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
Which position is preponderant? The Hanafi view is based on stronger precedent (athar) and istihsan (juristic preference), so it is the mufta bihi within the school.
Later Hanafi masters confirm this ruling in Zahir al-Riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن احتاج، قاتل بسلاحهم وخيلهم... وإن لم يحتاجوا إليها حبسها منهم كسائر أموالهم، والكراع أي الخيل يباع ويحبس ثمنه؛ لأنه أيسر؛ لاحتياجه إلى نفقة وقد تبلغ القيمة فكان بيعه أنفع لصاحبه، ولو كان معهم أهل الذمة يعينونهم على القتال، فحكمهم حكم أهل البغي حتى لا يجوز استرقاقهم، ولا أخذ أموالهم؛ لأن عهدهم لا ينتقض به
And if he (the Imam) needs, he fights with their weapons and horses... And if they do not need them, he holds them like the rest of their wealth. And the horses are sold and their price is held, because that is easier, due to the need for expenditure, and the value may be high, so selling is more beneficial for its owner. And if there are dhimmis with them assisting them in fighting, their ruling is the same as the rebels; they cannot be enslaved nor their wealth taken, because their covenant is not broken by this.
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage states the ruling of the Imam regarding the property of rebels (bughat) during battle. When there is need, the Imam may use their weapons and horses to fight them, as this is considered a loan or temporary use, not ownership. This is based on the precedent of Ali (RA) who distributed weapons among his companions at the Battle of Basra, and the Prophet (SAW) taking a shield from Safwan as a loan. If there is no need, the property is merely held back and not divided. The same applies to dhimmis fighting alongside rebels—they retain their dhimmi status. The proof is istihsan based on necessity and prophetic action.

Key Terms
بغاة (bughah): Rebels who have an interpretation (ta'wil) and are not simply bandits; they have a group and leader.
حاجة (hajah): Necessity in battle that justifies using rebel property without authorization.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When may the Imam use the weapons and horses of the rebels without their consent?
A) At any time
B) Only when necessary for battle
C) Never, because it is stealing
D) Only after they are defeated

Key Takeaway
Rebel property may be used in battle only when needed, reflecting the Hanafi principle of necessity overriding property rights in specific contexts.

Rebel-on-Rebel Killing: No Qisas or Diya
Ruling on rebel-on-rebel killing and the effect of rebel jurisdiction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that no qisas or diya is due when a rebel kills another rebel, and explain the condition that determines whether qisas applies when a citizen kills a rebel.

Core Idea
No worldly penalty for rebel-on-rebel killing; their blood is not protected during rebellion.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq clarifies a nuanced point in Islamic criminal law regarding rebels (bughat). It distinguishes between killings occurring within the rebel group and those involving citizens after the rebellion. The ruling hinges on the protected status of the victim and the jurisdiction of Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage presents two distinct cases: (1) a rebel kills another rebel (bagh mithlihi), and (2) a citizen kills a rebel after the rebels have taken over a city.
Focus on the shift from intra-rebel killing to citizen-rebel killing.
2
Apply the ruling to scenario 1
For rebel-on-rebel killing, no qisas or diya is due, regardless of intent (intentional or accidental). The victim's blood is not protected.
The word 'عمداً أو خطأ' covers both intentional and accidental.
3
Analyze scenario 2 with the condition
For a citizen killing a rebel after recapture, the ruling depends on whether the rebels had implemented their own laws. If they did, no worldly penalty; if they did not, qisas is due.
The conditional phrase 'فإن أجروا أحكامهم' is key.
4
Understand the rationale
The blood of rebels is not ma'sum during rebellion because they are akin to combatants. Implementation of rebel laws severs the jurisdiction of the just Imam, so qisas cannot be applied.
This is a rational proof (dalil aqli) based on the nature of rebellion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن قتل باغ مثله، فظهر عليهم، لم يجب شيء عمداً أو خطأ... وإن غلبوا على أهل مصر، فقتل مصري مثله، فظهر على المصر قتل به، وإن لم يجر أحكامهم، فإن أجروا أحكامهم لم يجب شيء، ولكن يستحق القاتل عذاب الآخرة.
And if a rebel kills someone like him (i.e., another rebel), then we prevail over them, nothing is required, whether intentionally or by mistake... And if they overpower the people of a city, and a citizen kills someone like him (i.e., a rebel), then if we prevail over the city, he is killed for it (qisas), if they had not implemented their laws. If they implemented their laws, nothing is required, but the killer deserves the punishment of the Hereafter.
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage distinguishes two scenarios. First: if a rebel kills another rebel while they are in rebellion, and then the just side prevails, no qisas or diya is due because the blood of rebels is not protected during their rebellion; they are like combatants. This applies whether the killing was intentional or accidental. Second: if rebels conquer a Muslim city and a citizen kills a rebel (or vice versa?), then if the just side recaptures the city before the rebels implement their own laws, qisas applies because the original Islamic law still governs. But if the rebels have implemented their laws, the Imam's authority has been effectively removed, so no qisas or diya is due—the killer only fears divine punishment. The rationale is that Islamic jurisdiction is necessary for qisas; without it, worldly penalties lapse.

Key Terms
باغ مثله (bagh mithlihi): A rebel killed by another rebel; both are part of the same rebel group.
أحكامهم (ahkamuhum): The laws implemented by the rebels in the territory they control.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a rebel kills another rebel during rebellion, what is the penalty?
A) Qisas
B) Diya
C) Nothing worldly
D) Both qisas and diya

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school exempts rebel-on-rebel killing from worldly penalty, but the presence or absence of rebel jurisdiction determines liability for citizen-on-rebel killings.

Inheritance When a Killer Claims Ta'wil
Intra-Hanafi dispute on inheritance when killer claims to be on the right.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on inheritance when a killer claims ta'wil and distinguish the intra-Hanafi disagreement between Abu Hanifa/Muhammad and Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
A killer who claims a valid interpretation (ta'wil) inherits in the Hanafi school — only unlawful killing bars inheritance.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute about inheritance when a person kills another but claims to be acting on a religious interpretation. The majority view (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) allows inheritance if the killer states he was on the right, while Abu Yusuf bars it absolutely. Understanding this difference clarifies the Hanafi concept of hirmān (deprivation of inheritance) and the role of ta'wil.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule
Identify the ruling given in the text: if a just person kills a rebel or vice versa, and the killer says "I was on the right," he inherits; if he says "I was on falsehood," he does not inherit.
The statement "I was on the right" indicates a claim of ta'wil.
2
Locate the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Note that Abu Yusuf disagrees entirely: he says the killer never inherits regardless of his claim. This is the opposing position within the Hanafi school.
Abu Yusuf's view is a minority position not relied upon for fatwa.
3
Analyse the majority proof
Examine the two arguments of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: (1) if the killer claims right, the killing is considered rightful, so liability (damān) is removed and inheritance should not be barred; (2) the hadith of al-Zuhri that the Companions agreed that bloodshed based on Qur'anic interpretation is waived, and by analogy, it does not prevent inheritance.
The false ta'wil is treated like true ta'wil when accompanied by might (man'ah) in defense, similar to the people of war not being liable for destruction.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The majority view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad is relied upon in the school. Al-Marghinani in Al-Hidayah prefers this view. Thus, the operative ruling is that the killer inherits if he claims ta'wil.
This is the position taught in most Hanafi texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن قتل عادل باغياً أو قتله باغ وقال: أنا على حق ورثه، وإن قال: أنا على باطل لم يرث. قال أبو يوسف: لا يرث مطلقاً. ولهما: أنه إذا قال: قتلته وأنا على حق يكون قتلاً بحق ألا ترى أنه يسقط به الضمان، فلا توجب به الحرمان. ولهما أيضاً: ما روى الزهري أن الصحابة اتفقوا على أن كل دم أريق بتأويل القرآن هو موضوع؛ لأنه أتلف تأويل فاسد والفاسد منه ملحق بالصحيح إذا ضمت إليه منعة في حق الدفع، ولهذا لا يضمن أهل الحرب ما تلفوا علينا.
And if a just person kills a rebel or a rebel kills him, and he (the killer) says: 'I was on the right,' he inherits. And if he says: 'I was on falsehood,' he does not inherit. Abu Yusuf said: He does not inherit absolutely. And the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) said: If he says 'I killed him and I was on the right,' it is a rightful killing. Do you not see that it removes liability (damān), so it should not cause deprivation (of inheritance). And they also said: What al-Zuhri narrated that the Companions agreed that every blood shed by interpretation of the Qur'an is waived, because it is damage by false interpretation, and the false is attached to the true when there is might in the context of defense. Therefore, the people of war are not liable for what they destroy of ours.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage discusses a dispute within the Hanafi school regarding inheritance when a just person kills a rebel or vice versa. The relied-upon position (of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) is that if the killer claims to be on the right (i.e., acting under a valid interpretation), he inherits from the deceased because the killing was not unlawful, thus it does not bar inheritance (hirmān). Abu Yusuf holds that inheritance is cut off entirely because the killing is still a prohibited act. The proof for the majority is the hadith of al-Zuhri that the Companions agreed that any bloodshed based on Qur'anic interpretation is waived (i.e., no worldly liability), and by analogy, it does not prevent inheritance. The principle: a killer with a valid ta'wil is like a warrior in dar al-harb who does not incur liability. The practical consequence: in most Hanafi fatwa, the killer inherits if he claims ta'wil.

Key Terms
حرمان (hirmān): Deprivation of inheritance due to killing.
تأويل (ta'wīl): Interpretation of religious texts that justifies action.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority Hanafi view (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), what happens to inheritance if a just person kills a rebel and says 'I was on the right'?
A) He inherits
B) He does not inherit
C) He inherits half
D) No ruling

Key Takeaway
A killer who claims ta'wil inherits in the Hanafi school, because the killing is considered rightful and does not bar inheritance.
